Two Doctrines on the Form of Compounds
Presentation of two doctrines on the form of compounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two alternative accounts of what happens to elemental forms when a compound is formed: the doctrine of stripping and intermediate form, and the doctrine of specific form.

Core Idea
The two doctrines both reject that elemental forms persist in compounds, but differ on what new form the compound acquires.

In classical Arabic philosophical chemistry, the concept of temperament (mizaj) describes the quality resulting from the interaction of elements. However, there is disagreement about what happens to the elemental forms themselves. This passage presents two doctrines that diverge from the standard view, which holds that elemental forms persist. Understanding these alternatives is crucial for grasping later refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall structure. Notice that it starts with a notice (تنبيه) and then presents two doctrines.
The passage uses 'الأول' and 'المذهب الثاني' to mark the two views.
2
Identify the first doctrine
The first doctrine states that the elements strip off their forms and the whole compound puts on a single intermediate form. Note the description: 'صورة متوسطة' between the opposed forms of the simples.
This is the 'stripping and replacement' view.
3
Identify the second doctrine
The second doctrine states that the compound puts on a specific form (صورة نوعية) that is not intermediate but rather a new specific form for the compound itself.
Note the use of 'بل' (rather) to contrast with the first.
4
Recognize the common ground and difference
Both doctrines agree with the majority on the resulting quality of temperament but disagree on the persistence of elemental forms. This is the key point that leads to refutation.
The passage says they 'يخالفهم في بقاء صور البسائط'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه على مذاهب في المزاج ) مخالفة لما مر (الأول : أنه يخلع صورة ويلبس صورة متوسطة ) العناصر إذا امتزجت وانفعل بعضها عن بعض أدى ذلك بها إلى أن يخلع صورها فلا يبقى لشيء منها صورته المخصوصة به ويلبس الكل حينئذ صورة واحدة هي في مادة واحدة وتلك الصورة متوسطة بين الصور المتضادة التي للبسائط . المذهب الثاني : ( بل يلبس صورة نوعية للمركب ) أي ليست الصورة الملبوسة صورة متوسطة بل هي صورة أخرى نوعية فالقائل بأحد هذين القولين يوافق الجمهور بحسب الظاهر في المزاج بالمعنى المذكور سابقاً لكنه يخالفهم في بقاء صور البسائط في المركبات ذوات الأمزجة، ويرد عليه أن ما ذكره فساد ما وكون لا مزاج لأنه إنما يكون عند بقاء الممتزجات بأعيانها ( ويبطله) أيضاً (ما حكيناه من حكايات القرع والإنبيق لأن اختلافها ما يظهر فيه أي في المركب ) من الأجزاء يدل على اختلاف الاستعداد فيها أي في تلك الأجزاء يعني أنا إذا وضعناه فيهما المركب كقطعة لحم مثلا يميز
يعني أن حالة أن يكون المعد نفس الكيفية لا أن يكون نفس الصورة كما ذكره المصنف بل المناسب لكون المعد نفس الصورة هو ما ذكره الشارح بقوله : وحينئذ يقول .
قوله : ( بأحد هذين القولين) أي اللذين ثانيهما هو الأول كما أشار إليه بكلمة بل . وقوله : في المزاج بالمعنى المذكور وهو الكيفية المتوسطة المتشابهة الحاصلة من تفاعل العناصر. وقوله : إن ما ذكره أي ذكره ذلك القائل بأحد القولين.
A notice on doctrines regarding the temperament, contrary to what has passed. First: That it strips off a form and puts on an intermediate form. The elements, when they mix and are acted upon by each other, that leads them to strip off their forms, so nothing of them retains its specific form, and the whole then puts on a single form which is in a single matter, and that form is intermediate between the opposed forms of the simples. The second doctrine: Rather, it puts on a specific form of the compound, i.e., the form put on is not an intermediate form but rather another specific form. So the proponent of either of these two statements agrees with the majority apparently regarding the temperament in the previously mentioned sense, but he disagrees with them regarding the persistence of the forms of the simples in compound have temperaments. And it is refuted by that what he mentioned is a corruption and that there is no temperament, because it only exists when the mixed things remain in their identities. And it is also invalidated by what we have narrated from the stories of the gourd and the alembic, because their difference appears in it, i.e., in the compound, of parts indicating differing preparedness in them, i.e., in those parts, meaning that if we place the compound, e.g., a piece of meat in them, it distinguishes...
Meaning that the state of the preparer being the quality itself, not the form itself, as the author mentioned; rather, what is appropriate for the preparer being the form itself is what the commentator said: And then he says.
His saying: (By one of these two statements) i.e., the second of which is the first, as he indicated by the word 'بل'. His saying: 'In the temperament in the previously mentioned sense' which is the intermediate similar quality resulting from the interaction of the elements. His saying: 'What he mentioned' i.e., mentioned by that proponent of one of the two statements.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a notice (تنبيه) presenting two alternative doctrines on what happens to elemental forms when a compound (مزاج) is formed, contrary to the earlier view. The first doctrine states that when elements mix and act on each other, they shed their individual forms and the whole compound acquires a single intermediate form that is between the opposed forms of the simples. The second doctrine says the compound acquires a new specific form (صورة نوعية) for the compound, not an intermediate one. Both doctrines agree with the majority opinion regarding the resulting quality of the temperament but disagree on whether the elemental forms persist. The author rejects these views: if the elements lose their forms, then there is no true mixture (مزاج), because mixture requires the persistence of the mixed components. Additionally, the differing parts observed in a compound (e.g., a piece of meat) show differing preparedness, which indicates that the elemental forms are still present in some way. The commentary then clarifies that the preparer (المعد) is the quality, not the form, and that the author's statement is about this distinction.

Key Terms
مزاج
صورة متوسطة
صورة نوعية
بسائط
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These doctrines are the standard view on what happens to elemental forms in a compound.
Correction: The passage explicitly says these are 'مخالفة لما مر' (contrary to what has passed), meaning they are alternatives to the standard view that elemental forms persist.

Checkpoint
According to the first doctrine presented, what happens to the elemental forms when a compound is formed?
A) They persist unchanged.
B) They are stripped off and replaced by an intermediate form.
C) They are transformed into a specific new form.
D) They disappear completely leaving no form.

Key Takeaway
Two alternative doctrines on the form of compounds both assert that elemental forms are lost, but disagree on whether the compound acquires an intermediate or a specific new form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The two doctrines both claim that elemental forms are lost upon mixing, but they differ on what replaces them. In your own understanding, is there a belief you hold that you know deviates from the majority position? Have you examined the basis of that belief as carefully as the author examines these doctrines?
?
The author presents these doctrines as alternatives that are rejected. Is there an area of your faith where you rely on a minority opinion without fully understanding its arguments or the reasons it is rejected by the majority? What steps can you take to gain certainty?

Refutation of the Two Doctrines: Forms Persist and the Preparer is Quality
Refutation of the two doctrines and clarification on the preparer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments against the two doctrines on the form of compounds, including the necessity of persistence for tempering and the evidence from differing preparedness.

Core Idea
If elemental forms were lost, there would be no true mixture, and differing parts in a compound prove that the forms still exist.

Having presented two alternative doctrines that deny the persistence of elemental forms in compounds, the author now refutes them. The refutation consists of two arguments: one logical and one empirical. The passage also clarifies the concept of 'preparer' (المعد) and its relation to the form and quality. This lesson unpacks these refutations and the correct understanding of temperament.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage starting from 'ويرد عليه' and its translation. Note the two parts of the refutation: a direct logical refutation and an empirical one based on stories of the gourd and alembic.
The passage uses 'ويرد عليه' and 'ويبطله أيضاً'.
2
Understand the first refutation
The first argument states that if the elemental forms are lost, there would be no true temperament (مزاج) because temperament requires the mixed things to remain in their identities. The view is dismissed as 'فساد وكون' (corruption and non-existence).
This is a logical argument based on the definition of mixture.
3
Understand the second refutation
The second argument refers to the stories of the gourd and alembic, which show that parts of a compound have differing preparedness (استعداد), indicating that the elemental forms must still be present in some way.
The translation is somewhat cryptic; it mentions placing a piece of meat in them.
4
Note the clarification about the preparer
The passage ends by clarifying that the preparer (المعد) is the quality itself, not the form, which is a correction of a potential misunderstanding from the author's earlier words.
This connects to the concept of preparedness (استعداد).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه على مذاهب في المزاج ) مخالفة لما مر (الأول : أنه يخلع صورة ويلبس صورة متوسطة ) العناصر إذا امتزجت وانفعل بعضها عن بعض أدى ذلك بها إلى أن يخلع صورها فلا يبقى لشيء منها صورته المخصوصة به ويلبس الكل حينئذ صورة واحدة هي في مادة واحدة وتلك الصورة متوسطة بين الصور المتضادة التي للبسائط . المذهب الثاني : ( بل يلبس صورة نوعية للمركب ) أي ليست الصورة الملبوسة صورة متوسطة بل هي صورة أخرى نوعية فالقائل بأحد هذين القولين يوافق الجمهور بحسب الظاهر في المزاج بالمعنى المذكور سابقاً لكنه يخالفهم في بقاء صور البسائط في المركبات ذوات الأمزجة، ويرد عليه أن ما ذكره فساد ما وكون لا مزاج لأنه إنما يكون عند بقاء الممتزجات بأعيانها ( ويبطله) أيضاً (ما حكيناه من حكايات القرع والإنبيق لأن اختلافها ما يظهر فيه أي في المركب ) من الأجزاء يدل على اختلاف الاستعداد فيها أي في تلك الأجزاء يعني أنا إذا وضعناه فيهما المركب كقطعة لحم مثلا يميز
يعني أن حالة أن يكون المعد نفس الكيفية لا أن يكون نفس الصورة كما ذكره المصنف بل المناسب لكون المعد نفس الصورة هو ما ذكره الشارح بقوله : وحينئذ يقول .
قوله : ( بأحد هذين القولين) أي اللذين ثانيهما هو الأول كما أشار إليه بكلمة بل . وقوله : في المزاج بالمعنى المذكور وهو الكيفية المتوسطة المتشابهة الحاصلة من تفاعل العناصر. وقوله : إن ما ذكره أي ذكره ذلك القائل بأحد القولين.
A notice on doctrines regarding the temperament, contrary to what has passed. First: That it strips off a form and puts on an intermediate form. The elements, when they mix and are acted upon by each other, that leads them to strip off their forms, so nothing of them retains its specific form, and the whole then puts on a single form which is in a single matter, and that form is intermediate between the opposed forms of the simples. The second doctrine: Rather, it puts on a specific form of the compound, i.e., the form put on is not an intermediate form but rather another specific form. So the proponent of either of these two statements agrees with the majority apparently regarding the temperament in the previously mentioned sense, but he disagrees with them regarding the persistence of the forms of the simples in compound have temperaments. And it is refuted by that what he mentioned is a corruption and that there is no temperament, because it only exists when the mixed things remain in their identities. And it is also invalidated by what we have narrated from the stories of the gourd and the alembic, because their difference appears in it, i.e., in the compound, of parts indicating differing preparedness in them, i.e., in those parts, meaning that if we place the compound, e.g., a piece of meat in them, it distinguishes...
Meaning that the state of the preparer being the quality itself, not the form itself, as the author mentioned; rather, what is appropriate for the preparer being the form itself is what the commentator said: And then he says.
His saying: (By one of these two statements) i.e., the second of which is the first, as he indicated by the word 'بل'. His saying: 'In the temperament in the previously mentioned sense' which is the intermediate similar quality resulting from the interaction of the elements. His saying: 'What he mentioned' i.e., mentioned by that proponent of one of the two statements.

Commentary
This passage is a notice (تنبيه) presenting two alternative doctrines on what happens to elemental forms when a compound (مزاج) is formed, contrary to the earlier view. The first doctrine states that when elements mix and act on each other, they shed their individual forms and the whole compound acquires a single intermediate form that is between the opposed forms of the simples. The second doctrine says the compound acquires a new specific form (صورة نوعية) for the compound, not an intermediate one. Both doctrines agree with the majority opinion regarding the resulting quality of the temperament but disagree on whether the elemental forms persist. The author rejects these views: if the elements lose their forms, then there is no true mixture (مزاج), because mixture requires the persistence of the mixed components. Additionally, the differing parts observed in a compound (e.g., a piece of meat) show differing preparedness, which indicates that the elemental forms are still present in some way. The commentary then clarifies that the preparer (المعد) is the quality, not the form, and that the author's statement is about this distinction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation is based on a simple rejection without evidence.
Correction: The author provides both a logical argument (definition of temperament) and an empirical argument (observation of differing preparedness).

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the implication of the differing preparedness observed in a compound?
A) The compound has no form.
B) The elemental forms must still be present.
C) The temperament is destroyed.
D) The preparer is the form.

Key Takeaway
The two doctrines are refuted because true mixture requires persistence of elements, and empirical evidence shows differing preparedness that implies persistence; moreover, the correct understanding is that the preparer is the quality, not the form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between the preparer being a quality versus a form. What is the 'quality' that prepares your heart to receive divine guidance? Are you actively cultivating that quality through consistent actions, or are you relying on outward forms (e.g., rituals without presence) hoping they will change you? Based on this lesson, what concrete step will you take today to realign your 'preparer'?
?
The scholar used empirical stories as evidence for the persistence of forms. In your spiritual life, what 'empirical evidence' do you have that your faith is genuine—something that endures through trials and changes? If you lack such evidence, what will you do to produce it, knowing that true mixture requires persistence?
?
The refutation argues that loss of forms leads to corruption and no true mixture. Consider your own character: are you trying to 'mix' good deeds with bad intentions such that your identity as a believer is corrupted? Or do you strive to persist in sincerity? What must you remove to ensure your spiritual 'mixture' is sound?

The Preparer of Cold: Quality, Not Substantial Form
Argument that quality is the preparatory cause for broken coldness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the quality, not the substantial form, is the preparatory cause (muʿidd) for the broken coldness, and how this relates to the broader debate about divine agency.

Core Idea
The quality (kayfiyya) prepares, the form (sura) only metaphorically acts.

This passage dives into a subtle but crucial distinction in Islamic cosmology: what exactly is the cause when a mixture produces a new quality? The author argues that it is the quality itself that prepares the matter to receive the new quality from the Active Intellect, not the substantial form. He defends this view against objections and connects it to the nature of the ultimate agent—free or necessary. Prepare to follow a chain of reasoning that touches on emanation, causality, and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author opens with a clear claim and then responds to an objection.
The passage uses technical terms like muʿidd and fāʿil.
2
Identify the main claim and objection
The author claims that the preparer (muʿidd) for broken coldness is the quality, not the form. Then he presents an objection: that 'agent' is used metaphorically for the form. Follow how he responds.
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قيل'.
3
Analyze the example of motion
The author gives the example of motion preparing arrival at an endpoint, even though motion and arrival cannot coexist. Understand why this supports his argument.
This shows the preparer may not accompany the effect.
4
Trace the broader dispute
At the end, the author frames the debate in terms of whether the principle is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) or necessary agent (mūjib bi-l-dhāt). This reframes the need for preparation.
The author promises to prove the principle is a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الأول : الصور الحالة في الجسم
لكسر البرودة هي الكيفية دون الصورة، فإن قيل: نحن نطلق عليها أي على الصورة الفاعل مجازاً لا حقيقة فإنها ليست موجدة للكيفية المنكسرة. وإنما ذلك أي الحاصل من الصورة إعداد لمادة المجاور لقبول الكيفية المنكسرة، وأما الكيفية المنكسرة المتوسطة فإنها تفيض على المركب عن مفيض المبدأ الفياض المسمى عندهم بالعقل الفعال. والمعد قد ينافي الأثر الصادر من الفاعل بتوسط إعداده كالحركة والحصول في الطرف من المسافة فإن الحركة معدة لذلك الحصول مع امتناع اجتماعهما. وحينئذ نقول: الصورة المائية بتوسط الحرارة العارضة تعد مادة الماء البارد لقبول الحرارة. وإن لم تكن تقتضيها بالذات، فإن هذا أهون من المنافاة بل إن جعل الكيفيات أنفسها معدة لمواد ما يضادها لم يلزم منه محال مما ذكر إذ المعد قد لا يجامع الأثر. قلنا: فالنزاع على هذا التقدير عائد إلى أن المبدأ فاعل مختار فلا حاجة إلى إعداد أو موجب بالذات فيتوقف تأثيره على الإعداد وسنقيم الدلالة على أنه فاعل مختار فيبطل القول بأن الصورة أو الكيفية معدة لصدور المزاج عن المبدأ.
The first observatory - the fourth section - the first aim: the forms (suwar) in the body. Regarding the breaking of coldness: it is the quality (al-kayfiyya), not the form (al-sura). If it is said: we apply the term 'agent (al-fāʿil)' to it (i.e., to the form) metaphorically, not really, because it does not create the broken quality. Rather, that (i.e., what results from the form) is a preparation (iʿdād) for the matter of the adjacent [element] to receive the broken quality. As for the intermediate broken quality, it emanates upon the composite from the principle of emanation, the gushing principle called by them the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl). The preparer (al-muʿidd) may contradict the effect emanating from the agent by means of its preparation, like motion and arriving at the extremity of the distance: motion prepares for that arrival while their coexistence is impossible. Then we say: the watery form, by means of the accidental heat, prepares the matter of cold water to receive heat, even if it does not necessitate it essentially, for this is easier than contradiction. Rather, if the qualities themselves are made to be preparers for matters that are opposite to them, no impossible consequence follows from what has been mentioned, since the preparer may not accompany the effect. We say: the dispute on this estimation returns to whether the principle is an agent by choice (fāʿil mukhtār), so there is no need for preparation, or an agent by essence (mūjib bi-l-dhāt), in which case its effect depends on preparation. We will establish the proof that it is an agent by choice, so the statement that the form or the quality is a preparer for the emanation of the mixture from the principle is invalidated.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the cause of the broken coldness (the intermediate quality resulting from mixture). The author argues that the preparatory cause (muʿidd) is the quality itself, not the substantial form. He first distinguishes between form and quality, noting that the agent is called agent metaphorically, not really, because it does not create the effect ex nihilo. The form only prepares the matter to receive the quality from the Active Intellect. The author then gives the example of motion: motion prepares arrival at the end point, yet motion and arrival are temporally distinct and cannot coexist. Similarly, the watery form, via accidental heat, prepares cold water to receive heat. No contradiction arises because the preparer may not accompany the effect. He then frames the dispute more broadly: if the principle is a free agent, preparation is unnecessary; if it is necessary by essence, preparation is required. The author promises proof that the principle is a free agent, thus refuting the view that forms or qualities are preparers for mixture.

Key Terms
المعد (al-muʿidd)
الصورة (al-ṣūra)
الكيفية (al-kayfiyya)
الفاعل المختار (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār)
الموجب بالذات (al-mūjib bi-l-dhāt)
العقل الفعال (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the substantial form directly creates the broken coldness.
Correction: The passage explains that the form only prepares the matter to receive the quality from the Active Intellect; the quality itself is the preparatory cause, and true agency belongs to the Active Intellect or ultimately to God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preparatory cause (muʿidd) for the broken coldness?
A) The substantial form (al-ṣūra)
B) The quality (al-kayfiyya)
C) The matter of the adjacent element
D) The Active Intellect

Key Takeaway
The quality is the preparer for cold's breakage; the form only metaphorically acts, and true agency belongs to the free-acting principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you planned and prepared extensively, but the result was opposite to what you expected. Did you, in your heart, attribute the outcome to your own efforts or to God's sole agency? How will you remind yourself today that your preparations only dispose, never cause?
?
The passage argues that the dispute over creation reduces to whether God is a free agent. In which areas of your life do you struggle most to believe that God is absolutely free—not bound by your 'preparations' or expectations? What is one step you can take today to surrender that area to His absolute freedom?

Causality and Created Things

5 min
First Doctrine on Mixture: Stripping and Cladding
Defines the first of three doctrines on mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of three alternative doctrines on mixture (mizāj), which holds that when elements mix, they lose their original forms and acquire a single intermediate form.

Core Idea
When elements mix, their forms are stripped and replaced by an intermediate form.

We now turn to a different perspective on mixture (mizāj). The first doctrine proposes a radical transformation: each element loses its own substantial form, and the whole composite takes on a single form that is intermediate between the forms of the original simples. This form belongs to the entire mixture, not to its parts. Let's examine the text to understand the process and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the basic claim.
2
Identify the key process
Notice the two verbs: 'strips a form' (yakhluʿu ṣūra) and 'clothes a form' (yalbasu ṣūra). This is the core mechanism.
3
Understand 'intermediate form'
The new form is described as 'intermediate' between the opposite forms of the simples. Reflect on what this means for the properties of the mixture.
For example, hot and cold produce a temperature that is neither hot nor cold but in between.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you have grasped the doctrine correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه على مذاهب في المزاج مخالفة لما مر
الأول: أنه يخلع صورة ويلبس صورة متوسطة. العناصر إذا امتزجت وانفعل بعضها عن بعض أدى ذلك بها إلى أن يخلع صورها فلا يبقى لشيء منها صورته المخصوصة به ويلبس الكل حينئذ صورة واحدة هي في مادة واحدة وتلك الصورة متوسطة بين الصور المتضادة التي للبسائط.
A note on doctrines concerning mixture (al-mizāj) contrary to what has passed. The first: that it strips a form and clothes an intermediate form. The elements, when they mix and some are affected by others, leads them to strip their forms so that nothing of them remains with its particular form, and all then clothe a single form that is in a single matter, and that form is intermediate between the opposite forms that belong to the simples (al-basāʾiṭ).
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage introduces three alternative doctrines on mixture (mizāj). The first doctrine states that when elements (al-ʿanāṣir) mix and interact, they lose (strip) their original substantial forms (ṣuwar) and instead acquire one single form that is intermediate between the opposite forms of the simple elements. For example, when hot and cold mix, the resulting form is neither hot nor cold but an intermediate quality. This intermediate form belongs to the whole composite, not to individual parts. The author notes that this is contrary to the earlier view (which held that the qualities themselves are the preparers). The passage is definitional.

Key Terms
المزاج (al-mizāj)
يخلع صورة (yakhlūʿu ṣūra)
يلبس صورة (yalbasu ṣūra)
الصورة المتوسطة (al-ṣūra al-mutawassiṭa)
البسائط (al-basāʾiṭ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The original forms are simply overlaid or combined.
Correction: The first doctrine holds that the original forms are completely annihilated (stripped) and replaced by a single intermediate form.

Checkpoint
According to the first doctrine, what happens to the forms of elements when they mix?
A) They remain intact but are arranged differently
B) They are stripped and replaced by an intermediate form
C) They are transformed into a new form specific to the compound
D) They are combined into a mixture without change

Key Takeaway
The first doctrine on mixture teaches that when elements mix, they strip their original forms and acquire a single intermediate form that unifies the compound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the doctrine's description of mixture: elements strip their forms and clothe an intermediate form. Think of a significant internal conflict you have faced—opposing desires or beliefs within you. How did that conflict resolve? Was it by eliminating one side completely, or by finding an 'intermediate' stance that integrated both? What does your answer reveal about the nature of personal change according to this lesson?
?
The text says the intermediate form is 'in a single matter' after the mixture. Have you ever experienced a moment where your identity felt unified despite containing many influences? How does this doctrine challenge you to see that unity not as a collection of parts but as a new single reality?
?
If you were to apply this doctrine to a relationship where two people 'mix' their lives together, do you think each must lose their individual 'form' to form a new unity? What part of you would be 'stripped' and what 'clothed'? How does this lesson inform how you approach deep connections with others?

The Second Doctrine on Mixture: Refutation by Empirical and Logical Arguments
Presents and refutes the second doctrine on mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second doctrine on mixture (that a new specific form emerges) is invalid, based on empirical evidence from alchemy and logical reasoning about dispositions indicating distinct essences.

Core Idea
Alchemical experiments show that simple components persist, so no new species form arises.

This lesson dives into the second doctrine, which claims that when elements mix, they acquire a completely new specific form rather than an intermediate quality. The author refutes this by both logical analysis of what true mixture requires and by citing experiments with the gourd and alembic. As you read, notice how empirical evidence is used to ground the theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the doctrine statement
Read the opening line where the second doctrine is presented: 'بل يلبس صورة نوعية للمركب' – the compound clothes a new specific form.
Contrast this with the first doctrine (intermediate quality).
2
Identify the refutation's two parts
The refutation begins by saying this is not true mixture because mixture requires persistence of the mixed things. Then it introduces the alchemical experiment to show different dispositions.
The experiment is key empirical evidence.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Read the objection: if fieriness weakens in the compound and becomes meat, why cannot pure fire become meat directly? The answer: composition is a necessary condition.
The author also turns the objection against the opponent.
4
Reflect on the counter-argument
Notice how the author points out that the opponent's view also allows a simple to transform alone, so they cannot insist on composition being necessary.
This strengthens the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الثاني : ( بل يلبس صورة نوعية للمركب ) أي ليست الصورة الملبوسة صورة متوسطة بل هي صورة أخرى نوعية فالقائل بأحد هذين القولين يوافق الجمهور بحسب الظاهر في المزاج بالمعنى المذكور سابقاً لكنه يخالفهم في بقاء صور البسائط في المركبات ذوات الأمزجة، ويرد عليه أن ما ذكره فساد ما وكون لا مزاج لأنه إنما يكون عند بقاء الممتزجات بأعيانها ( ويبطله) أيضاً (ما حكيناه من حكايات القرع والإنبيق لأن اختلافها ما يظهر فيه أي في المركب ) من الأجزاء يدل على اختلاف الاستعداد فيها أي في تلك الأجزاء يعني أنا إذا وضعناه فيهما المركب كقطعة لحم مثلا يميز إلى جسم مائي متقاطر وإلى كلس أرضي لا يتقاطر، فدل ذلك على أن الأجزاء التي في المركب مختلفة في استعداد التقطير وعدمه، إذ لو كانت متفقة فيه لكان الكل قاطراً أو غير قاطر. وهو أي اختلاف الاستعداد دليل اختلاف الماهية لأن القابلية من لوازمها واختلاف اللوازم يدل على اختلاف الملزومات. وإنما لم نقل أن تلك الحكاية تدل على وجود صور البسائط في المركبات وإلا لم تنحل إليها احترازاً عن أن يقال: إنها تكونت بتأثير الحرارة إلا أنها كانت فيه. فإن قيل: إذا كان جوهر البسائط باقياً في المركب كانت النارية موجودة فيه لكنها مفترة في حرارتها، والصورة النوعية للمركب كاللحمة مثلا حاصلة في جميع أجزائه فتكون النارية التي عرض لها فتور في المركب قد صارت لحماً وإذا جاز ذلك فليجز في النار الصرفة المنفردة عن أخواتها أن تحدث لها الكيفية المتوسطة أي الحرارة المفترة فتصير لحما فلا يكون إلى التركيب والمزاج حاجة في حدوث الصور النوعية التي للمركبات. قلنا: المزاج أي التركيب شرط فيه أي ليس مجرد الاستحالة إلى الحرارة المفترة كافياً في حصول تلك الصورة النوعية بل لا بد مع الاستحالة من التركيب على أن هذه الشبهة واردة عليكم أيضاً، لأن خلع البسائط صورها ولبسها صوراً أخرى إنما يكون عند انتهاء كيفياتها إلى حد معين فمن الجائز أن تنتهي كيفية كل واحدة منها حال انفرادها إلى ذلك الحد حتى يفسد عنها صورتها وتحدث فيها الصورة المزاجية. ولا مفر لكم أيضاً سوى ما ذكرناه من اشتراط التركيب.
The second doctrine: rather it clothes a specific (nawʿī) form for the compound. Meaning the clothed form is not intermediate but another species form. The proponent of either of these two doctrines outwardly agrees with the majority concerning mixture in the previously mentioned sense, but disagrees with them regarding the persistence of the forms of the simples in compounds possessing mixtures. It is refuted: that what he mentioned is corrupted and is not mixture, because mixture only occurs with the persistence of the mixed things as individuals. And it is also invalidated by what we have narrated of the stories of the gourd and the alembic (al-qarʿ wa-l-anbīq), because their difference in what appears in the compound from its parts indicates a difference of disposition (ikhtilāf al-istiʿdād) in those parts. That is, if we place a compound, like a piece of meat, in them, it separates into a watery body that drips (mutaqāṭir) and an earthy lime (kals) that does not drip. That indicates that the parts of the compound differ in the disposition for dripping and its absence, because if they were uniform in that, all would be dripping or not dripping. This difference in disposition is evidence of difference in essence (māhiyya), because receptivity (qābiliyya) is among the concomitants of essence, and difference of concomitants indicates difference of the essences. We did not say that that story indicates the existence of the forms of the simples in the compounds, otherwise they would not dissolve into them, as a precaution against the claim that they are generated by the effect of heat. If it is said: If the substance of the simples remains in the compound, then the fieriness (nārīyya) is present in it but is weak (muftara) in its heat, and the specific form of the compound (like meat) is present in all its parts, so the fieriness that experienced weakness in the compound has become meat. If that is allowed, then it should be allowed in pure fire, isolated from its sisters, that the intermediate quality (i.e., weak heat) could arise in it, and it would become meat. Then there would be no need for composition and mixture in the generation of specific forms of compounds. We say: mixture, i.e., composition, is a condition for it, i.e., mere transformation into weak heat is not sufficient for the attainment of that specific form; rather, along with transformation, composition is necessary. Moreover, this sophism also applies to you, because the stripping of the simples' forms and their clothing other forms only occurs when their qualities reach a certain limit. It is possible that the quality of each one of them, in its isolated state, might reach that limit such that its form is corrupted and the form of mixture is generated in it. You have no escape except what we mentioned, the requirement of composition.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This lesson presents the second doctrine (that mixture yields a new specific form for the compound) and then refutes it. The refutation comes in two parts. First, a logical argument: what the doctrine describes is not true mixture because true mixture requires the persistence of the individual components. Second, an empirical argument from alchemical experiments: when a compound like meat is distilled in a gourd and alembic, it separates into a watery part that drips and an earthy part that does not drip, showing that the original simple components (water and earth) have different dispositions. This difference in disposition evidences a difference in essences, implying that the original forms of the simples are still present in the compound. The author addresses an objection: if the fieriness in the compound is weakened, then why can't pure fire become meat by simple weakening? The answer is that mixture (composition) is a necessary condition; mere qualitative change is insufficient. The author further turns the objection against the opponent: if the opponent claims that forms of simples are lost when their qualities reach a limit, then that limit could be reached in isolation without composition, so their theory also fails. Thus, the second doctrine is refuted.

Key Terms
الصورة النوعية (al-ṣūra al-nawʿiyya)
الاستعداد (al-istiʿdād)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)
الكيفية المتوسطة (al-kayfiyya al-mutawassiṭa)
التركيب (al-tarkīb)
المزاج (al-mizāj)
حد الاستحالة (ḥadd al-istiḥāla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The alchemical experiment proves the existence of the original forms of the simples in the compound.
Correction: The author explicitly says: 'We did not say that that story indicates the existence of the forms of the simples in the compounds.' It only proves different dispositions.

Checkpoint
What do the stories of the gourd and alembic prove according to the refuter?
A) That mixture yields a new specific form
B) That the compound contains parts with different dispositions
C) That qualities in the compound are intermediate
D) That fire can become meat without composition

Key Takeaway
The second doctrine on mixture is refuted because true mixture requires persistence of simples, and empirical evidence shows that components retain distinct essences, not a new single form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought you could become a completely new person by discarding your past, ignoring the ‘simple elements’ that still persist? What does this passage’s insistence on the persistence of simples teach you about your own identity and change?
?
When you combine acts of worship (prayer, fasting, charity) into a ‘spiritual life’, do you preserve their distinct essences or do you let them blur into a vague feeling? The distillation shows distinct parts – how will you ensure each component retains its specific form and intention?
?
The objection suggests that if fire can become meat by mere qualitative change, no composition is needed. In your spiritual purification, are you tempted to seek a shortcut—a transformation without proper composition of means and conditions? What is the ‘composition’ you must attend to?

The Khalit Theory: Mixture of Minuscule Parts
Defines the third doctrine on mixture: al-khalīṭ theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doctrine on mixture (al-khalīṭ), which holds that compounds consist of actual minuscule parts of different natures that intermix without qualitative change.

Core Idea
True natures never change—they only hide or appear.

The third theory of mixture, known as al-khalīṭ, offers a radical alternative to the idea of genuine transformation. According to this view, every compound contains tiny particles each retaining its own nature. When many particles of the same type gather, we sense that nature; otherwise, they remain hidden. This lesson unpacks the doctrine and its explanation of apparent change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage describes the third doctrine as a detailed version of the first.
2
Identify the core claim about natures and qualities
The passage states that in bodies there are parts on different natures (meat, wheat, atom) mixed together. There is no change in nature or qualities. Locate these claims.
The key phrase: 'فليس هناك تغير في الطبيعة، وكذا لا تغير في الكيفيات'
3
Analyze the example of heated water
The passage uses water heating as evidence. Water does not change its quality; latent fiery parts emerge upon contact with fire. Understand this as a denial of real transformation.
Contrast with the view that fiery parts enter from outside.
4
Note the variant opinion about external penetration
A group held that the fiery parts were not latent but penetrated from outside. This shows the theory has two branches.
The passage ends abruptly, but this variant is part of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الثالث وقد يجعل هذا مذهباً ثالثاً نظراً إلى تفصيل المذهب الأول كما أشرنا إليه ( القول بالخليط وهو أن المركبات موجودة بالفعل وقد يجتمع أجزاء منها فيحس لها قدر وإلا فلا يحس ) . فإن القائل بالخليط يزعم أن في الأجسام أجزاء على طبيعة اللحم وأجزاء على طبيعة الحنطة وأجزاء على طبيعة الذرة وهكذا. وهي متصغرة مختلطة جذاً فإذا اجتمع أجزاء كثيرة متجانسة أحس بها على تلك الطبيعة فليس هناك تغير في الطبيعة، وكذا لا تغير في الكيفيات فالماء إذا تسخن لم يستحل في كيفيته بل كان فيه أجزاء نارية كامنة فبرزت بملاقاة النار. وذهب جماعة إلى أن الأجزاء النارية لم تكن كامنة بل نفذت في الماء من خارج فهؤلاء أصحاب
The third doctrine: and this may be made a third doctrine due to a detailing of the first doctrine, as we have pointed to: the position on al-khalīṭ (mixture), which is that compounds exist in actuality, and parts of them may come together and a size (qadr) of them is sensed, otherwise it is not sensed. For the proponent of al-khalīṭ claims that in bodies there are parts on the nature of meat, parts on the nature of wheat, parts on the nature of an atom (dhurra), and so on. They are very small (mutaṣaghghira) and mixed (mukhtaliṭa). When many homogeneous parts come together, they are sensed according to that nature. So there is no change in nature, and likewise no change in qualities. So water, when heated, does not change its quality, but rather there were fiery parts latent (kāmina) in it, which emerged upon contact with fire. And a group went that the fiery parts were not latent but penetrated into the water from outside – these are the proponents...
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage describes the third doctrine on mixture, known as al-khalīṭ (or the theory of composition through intermingling of minuscule particles). According to this view, compounds do not undergo any real change in their natures or qualities. Instead, bodies contain extremely small parts each retaining its own nature (such as flesh-nature, wheat-nature, atom-nature). These parts are intermixed. When many parts of the same nature come together in sufficient quantity, they become perceptible and give the appearance of a pure substance. For example, when water is heated, it does not actually become hot water; rather, latent fiery parts within the water become manifest due to contact with external fire. The author then notes a variation: some thinkers say the fiery parts are not latent in the water but enter from outside. The third doctrine thus denies any genuine qualitative transformation; apparent change is due to assembly or separation of pre-existing homoeomeric particles.

Key Terms
الخليط (al-khalīṭ)
المركبات (al-murakkabāt)
الأجزاء المتصغرة (al-ajzāʾ al-mutaṣaghghira)
الطبيعة (al-ṭabīʿa)
الكيفية (al-kayfiyya)
الأجزاء النارية (al-ajzāʾ al-nāriyya)
الكامنة (al-kāmina)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The khalit theory is the same as atomism, positing undifferentiated atoms.
Correction: The khalit theory posits particles of specific natures (meat, wheat, etc.), not undifferentiated atoms.

Checkpoint
According to the khalit theory, what happens when water is heated?
A) The water's quality changes from cold to hot
B) Latent fiery parts in the water become manifest
C) The water is transformed into a new intermediate quality
D) The water evaporates completely

Key Takeaway
The khalit theory denies qualitative change; apparent change is due to assembly or separation of pre-existing homoeomeric particles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The khalit theory teaches that latent parts within you—like the fiery parts in water—only emerge when appropriate conditions arise. What hidden capacities or weaknesses might be latent within your own soul that you have not yet confronted? What 'fire' (a trial, a blessing, a challenge) might expose them, and how are you preparing yourself for that moment?
?
If your true nature is fixed and unchanging (as the theory posits for fundamental parts), then every change you experience in your faith, character, or desires is merely a recombination of what is already there. This means you cannot blame circumstances for creating new flaws or virtues; they only bring out what was already present. What does this accountability demand of you right now? Are you willing to examine what is truly in your heart, even the parts you keep hidden?

Proof That Differing Readiness Implies Differing Essences
Establishing the proof that differing readiness indicates differing essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the difference in readiness for dripping among parts of a compound proves that those parts have different essences.

Core Idea
Receptivity is a necessary property, so different receptivity means different essence.

The author presents a simple but powerful observation: if all parts of a compound had the same readiness to drip, the whole would be uniformly dripping or non-dripping. But we see variation, which means the parts must differ in their intrinsic nature. This lesson walks through that proof and its logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the Arabic passage (from فدل ذلك to دليل اختلاف الماهية). Notice the conditional structure and the conclusion.
The passage uses logical deduction; pay attention to 'لو' (if) and 'ل' (then).
2
Understand the observation
The author assumes that if all parts had the same readiness to drip, the entire compound would be either all dripping or all non-dripping. Since we observe that some parts drip and others do not, the parts cannot have the same readiness.
3
Grasp the logical step to essence
Readiness (استعداد) is a necessary property (لازم) of the essence (ماهية). If necessary properties differ, then the essences to which they belong must differ. Therefore, differing readiness implies differing essences.
This is a key principle of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فدل ذلك على أن الأجزاء التي في المركب مختلفة في استعداد التقطير ،وعدمه، إذ لو كانت متفقة فيه لكان الكل قاطراً أو غير قاطر . ( وهو) أي اختلاف الاستعداد دليل اختلاف الماهية لأن القابلية من لوازمها واختلاف اللوازم يدل على اختلاف الملزومات وإنما لم نقل أن تلك الحكاية تدل على وجود صور البسائط في المركبات وإلا لم تنحل إليها احترازاً عن أن يقال: إنها تكونت بتأثير الحرارة إلا أنها كانت فيه فإن قيل : إذا كان جوهر البسائط باقياً في المركب كانت النارية موجودة فيه لكنها مفترة في حرارتها، والصورة النوعية للمركب كاللحمة مثلا حاصلة في جميع أجزائه فتكون النارية التي عرض لها فتور في المركب قد صارت لحماً وإذا جاز ذلك ( فليجز في النار الصرفة ) المنفردة عن أخواتها ( أن تحدث لها الكيفية المتوسطة ) أي الحرارة المفترة ( فتصير لحما) فلا يكون إلى التركيب والمزاج حاجة في حدوث الصور النوعية التي للمركبات : قلنا : (المزاج أي التركيب ( شرط فيه أي ليس مجرد الاستحالة إلى الحرارة المفترة كافياً في حصول تلك الصورة النوعية بل لا بد مع الاستحالة من التركيب على أن هذه الشبهة واردة عليكم أيضاً، لأن خلع البسائط صورها ولبسها صوراً أخرى إنما يكون عند انتهاء كيفياتها إلى حد معين فمن الجائز أن تنتهي كيفية كل واحدة منها حال انفرادها إلى ذلك الحد حتى يفسد عنها صورتها وتحدث فيها الصورة المزاجية. ولا مفر لكم أيضاً سوى ما ذكرناه من اشتراط التركيب .
This indicates that the parts which are in the compound are different in readiness to drip and its absence, for if they were uniform in it, the whole would be dripping or non-dripping. And it (i.e., the difference in readiness) is evidence of difference in essence, because receptivity is among its necessary properties, and difference in necessary properties indicates difference in the essences to which they belong. And we did not say that that story indicates the existence of the forms of the simple elements in the compounds, otherwise they would not resolve into them, as a precaution against it being said: they were produced by the effect of heat, except that they were in it. For if it is said: If the substance of the simple elements remains in the compound, then fire-ness exists in it but it is flabby in its heat, and the specific form of the compound, like flesh for example, is present in all its parts, so the fire-ness which has suffered flabbiness in the compound has become flesh, and if that is permissible, then let it be permissible for pure fire—separate from its sisters—that a medium quality (i.e., flabby heat) should arise in it so that it becomes flesh, and there would be no need for composition and mixture in the generation of the specific forms of compounds. We said: Mixture (i.e., composition) is a condition in it, meaning that mere transformation into flabby heat is not sufficient for obtaining that specific form; rather, along with transformation, composition is necessary. Moreover, this doubt also applies to you, because the simple elements' discarding of their forms and putting on other forms only occurs when their qualities reach a certain limit; so it is possible that the quality of each one of them in its isolation reaches that limit such that its form corrupts and the mixture-form occurs in it. And you have no escape except what we mentioned, namely the condition of composition.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the difference in readiness for dripping among parts of a compound indicates a difference in their essences. The author first states the observation: if parts were uniform in their readiness to drip, the whole compound would be either entirely dripping or entirely non-dripping, but we see variation, so the parts must have different essences. He then explains that receptivity is a necessary property (lazima) of the essence, so differing receptivity implies differing essences. He then anticipates an objection: if the simple forms (like fire) remain in the compound, why can't fire alone, when its heat is flabby, become flesh without needing mixture? The author answers that mixture (composition) is a necessary condition; mere transformation of heat is insufficient. He further argues that the same principle applies to the objector's own view: simple elements can only change forms when their qualities reach a certain limit, and it is possible for them to reach that limit in isolation, so they too would need composition. Thus the proof stands.

Key Terms
استعداد
قابلية
ماهية
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author use as evidence for the difference in essences of the parts of a compound?
A) The difference in their sizes
B) The difference in their readiness to drip
C) The difference in their colors
D) The difference in their motion

Key Takeaway
Difference in necessary properties (like readiness) directly indicates difference in essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a repeated pattern in your life—a habit, a reaction, or a desire—that you have dismissed as 'just the way I am.' Based on this lesson, if that pattern is truly a necessary property of your essence, it cannot change. Does acknowledging its permanence terrify you or bring you peace? What if, instead, it is not essential, and you have the power to transform it? Will you examine it today?
?
The proof shows that different necessary properties indicate different essences. If you could strip away every accidental quality—your job, your appearance, your reputation—what remains? What one unchanging thing would you want to find at your core? Is it there? If not, what must you do to build it?
?
When you witness injustice in the world, do you attribute it to the essence of human nature ('people are just selfish') or to accidental circumstances? This lesson teaches that necessary properties reveal essence, and different properties mean different essences. If you believe that evil is essential to some people, what does that imply about their humanity? Will you let that belief shape how you treat them, or will you search for the accidental factors that can change?

Answering the Objection: Fire Becoming Flesh Without Mixture
Responding to the objection that fire could become flesh without mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere transformation of heat is insufficient for generating a compound form like flesh, and why composition is a necessary condition.

Core Idea
Mixture is a condition; a single element cannot become a compound by itself.

An objector argues that if fire in a mixture can become 'flabby' and turn into flesh, then pure fire should be able to do the same without composition. The author answers that composition itself is a necessary condition, not just a change in quality. This lesson examines that rebuttal and the counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic from 'فإن قيل' to the end. Notice the structure: objection (qil), then answer (qulna).
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims that if fire in the compound can have its heat reduced and become flesh, then pure fire, if its heat is reduced to the same degree, should also become flesh without need for mixture.
3
Grasp the author's answer
The author says that mixture (mizaj) is a condition, not just the flabbiness of heat. Even if the heat changes, the specific form of flesh requires the combination of multiple elements, not just one.
He also points out that the objector's own view faces the same problem: if simple elements change forms when their qualities reach a limit, they could change in isolation, so they too need composition.
4
Reflect on the implication
This defense shows that composition is essential for compound forms; the proof from readiness stands because it does not rely on the assumption of simple forms remaining intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فدل ذلك على أن الأجزاء التي في المركب مختلفة في استعداد التقطير ،وعدمه، إذ لو كانت متفقة فيه لكان الكل قاطراً أو غير قاطر . ( وهو) أي اختلاف الاستعداد دليل اختلاف الماهية لأن القابلية من لوازمها واختلاف اللوازم يدل على اختلاف الملزومات وإنما لم نقل أن تلك الحكاية تدل على وجود صور البسائط في المركبات وإلا لم تنحل إليها احترازاً عن أن يقال: إنها تكونت بتأثير الحرارة إلا أنها كانت فيه فإن قيل : إذا كان جوهر البسائط باقياً في المركب كانت النارية موجودة فيه لكنها مفترة في حرارتها، والصورة النوعية للمركب كاللحمة مثلا حاصلة في جميع أجزائه فتكون النارية التي عرض لها فتور في المركب قد صارت لحماً وإذا جاز ذلك ( فليجز في النار الصرفة ) المنفردة عن أخواتها ( أن تحدث لها الكيفية المتوسطة ) أي الحرارة المفترة ( فتصير لحما) فلا يكون إلى التركيب والمزاج حاجة في حدوث الصور النوعية التي للمركبات : قلنا : (المزاج أي التركيب ( شرط فيه أي ليس مجرد الاستحالة إلى الحرارة المفترة كافياً في حصول تلك الصورة النوعية بل لا بد مع الاستحالة من التركيب على أن هذه الشبهة واردة عليكم أيضاً، لأن خلع البسائط صورها ولبسها صوراً أخرى إنما يكون عند انتهاء كيفياتها إلى حد معين فمن الجائز أن تنتهي كيفية كل واحدة منها حال انفرادها إلى ذلك الحد حتى يفسد عنها صورتها وتحدث فيها الصورة المزاجية. ولا مفر لكم أيضاً سوى ما ذكرناه من اشتراط التركيب .
This indicates that the parts which are in the compound are different in readiness to drip and its absence, for if they were uniform in it, the whole would be dripping or non-dripping. And it (i.e., the difference in readiness) is evidence of difference in essence, because receptivity is among its necessary properties, and difference in necessary properties indicates difference in the essences to which they belong. And we did not say that that story indicates the existence of the forms of the simple elements in the compounds, otherwise they would not resolve into them, as a precaution against it being said: they were produced by the effect of heat, except that they were in it. For if it is said: If the substance of the simple elements remains in the compound, then fire-ness exists in it but it is flabby in its heat, and the specific form of the compound, like flesh for example, is present in all its parts, so the fire-ness which has suffered flabbiness in the compound has become flesh, and if that is permissible, then let it be permissible for pure fire—separate from its sisters—that a medium quality (i.e., flabby heat) should arise in it so that it becomes flesh, and there would be no need for composition and mixture in the generation of the specific forms of compounds. We said: Mixture (i.e., composition) is a condition in it, meaning that mere transformation into flabby heat is not sufficient for obtaining that specific form; rather, along with transformation, composition is necessary. Moreover, this doubt also applies to you, because the simple elements' discarding of their forms and putting on other forms only occurs when their qualities reach a certain limit; so it is possible that the quality of each one of them in its isolation reaches that limit such that its form corrupts and the mixture-form occurs in it. And you have no escape except what we mentioned, namely the condition of composition.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the difference in readiness for dripping among parts of a compound indicates a difference in their essences. The author first states the observation: if parts were uniform in their readiness to drip, the whole compound would be either entirely dripping or entirely non-dripping, but we see variation, so the parts must have different essences. He then explains that receptivity is a necessary property (lazima) of the essence, so differing receptivity implies differing essences. He then anticipates an objection: if the simple forms (like fire) remain in the compound, why can't fire alone, when its heat is flabby, become flesh without needing mixture? The author answers that mixture (composition) is a necessary condition; mere transformation of heat is insufficient. He further argues that the same principle applies to the objector's own view: simple elements can only change forms when their qualities reach a certain limit, and it is possible for them to reach that limit in isolation, so they too would need composition. Thus the proof stands.

Correction: The author argues that composition (mixture) is a necessary condition; mere transformation of heat without combining with other elements cannot produce a compound form.

The Khalit Theory of Mixture
The author describes the khalit theory and its two sub-positions on how fire enters water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the khalit theory of mixture, its two sub-positions, and how it denies transformation and generation.

Core Idea
Mixture is not transformation; it is the emergence or penetration of pre-existing parts.

This passage introduces a third theory of mixture called al-khalit, which argues that compounds are not generated from simpler elements but rather exist actually in minute mixed parts. Two versions are presented: latency-emergence and penetration-entry. Both reject the idea of transformation (istihala) and generation (kawn). The passage also connects this view to the broader concept of mizaj.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two groups mentioned and the key terms for each.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two sub-positions
Look for 'أصحاب الكمون والبروز' and 'أصحاب الغشو والنفوذ'. Understand how each explains the presence of fire in water.
3
Understand the denial of transformation
The passage explicitly says both groups deny 'الاستحالة' (transformation) and 'الكون' (generation). Why do they reject these?
This is central to their theory.
4
Connect to the view of mizaj
The last sentence relates the khalit theory to the view of mizaj. Note two different bases for mizaj corresponding to the two sub-positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب (الثالث ) وقد يجعل هذا مذهباً ثالثاً نظراً إلى تفصيل المذهب الأول كما أشرنا إليه ( القول بالخليط وهو أن المركبات موجودة بالفعل وقد يجتمع أجزاء منها فيحس لها قدر وإلا فلا يحس ) . فإن القائل بالخليط يزعم أن في الأجسام أجزاء على طبيعة اللحم وأجزاء على طبيعة الحنطة وأجزاء على طبيعة الذرة وهكذا. وهي متصغرة مختلطة جذاً فإذا اجتمع أجزاء كثيرة متجانسة أحس بها على تلك الطبيعة فليس هناك تغير في الطبيعة، وكذا لا تغير في الكيفيات فالماء إذا تسخن لم يستحل في كيفيته بل كان فيه أجزاء نارية كامنة فبرزت بملاقاة النار. وذهب جماعة إلى أن الأجزاء النارية لم تكن كامنة بل نفذت في الماء من خارج فهؤلاء أصحاب الغشو والنفوذ والأولون أصحاب الكمون والبروذ وكلاهما ينكران الاستحالة والكون. والقول بالمزاج مبني على القول بهما أما على الأول فلان حصول المزاج باستحالة الأركان كما عرفت . وأما على الثاني فلان النار لا تهبط عن الأثير بل تتكون هاهنا .
The third view (and this may be considered a third view in light of the elaboration of the first view as we have indicated) is the statement of mixture (al-khalit), which is that compounds exist in actuality, and when parts of them come together, a sense of them is perceived, otherwise not. For the proponent of the khalit claims that in bodies there are parts of the nature of flesh, parts of the nature of wheat, parts of the nature of an atom, and so on. They are minute and thoroughly mixed. When many homogeneous parts come together, they are sensed as that nature. So there is no change in nature, and likewise no change in qualities. Thus water, when heated, does not change in its quality; rather, it had fire parts latent in it, and they emerged upon contact with fire. Another group held that the fire parts were not latent but rather penetrated into the water from outside. These are the proponents of penetration and entry, while the former are the proponents of latency and emergence. Both deny transformation and generation. The view of mixture (mizaj) is built upon these two views. As for the first, the occurrence of mixture is through the transformation of the elements, as you have learned. As for the second, fire does not descend from the ether but rather is generated here.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents the third school of thought on the nature of mixtures. According to this view, called 'al-khalit' (the mixture), compounds are not generated from simpler elements through transformation; rather, they always exist actually, composed of minuscule parts of each nature (e.g., flesh parts, wheat parts) that are mixed together. When enough homogeneous parts accumulate, we sense the corresponding nature. Thus, when water is heated, it is not that water transforms into something else; rather, latent fire parts within it emerge due to the external fire. The author then distinguishes two subgroups: one holds that these fire parts are latent (kumun) and emerge (buruz), while the other holds that they are not latent but rather penetrate (nufudh) from outside. Both groups reject transformation (istihala) and generation (kawn). Finally, the author relates this to the view of mixture (mizaj) which is built upon either of these two positions: either mixture occurs through transformation of elements, or it occurs through generation of fire here below.

كمون
بروز
غشو
نفوذ
استحالة
كون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The khalit theory is the same as the view that mixture involves transformation (istihala).
Correction: The khalit theory explicitly denies transformation; it posits that mixture occurs through the combination of pre-existing parts, not through the change of one substance into another.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The khalit theory maintains that mixture is the combination of pre-existing parts, not a transformation of elements, and it denies generation.

?
Both khalit sub-positions reject that anything truly new comes into existence (kawn). In your own life, do you act as if real newness is possible—new habits, new faith, new character—or do you secretly believe you are stuck with only what is latent? The Truly Temperate: Balancing Quantity and Quality
The author defines the truly temperate mixture and states the conditions for its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the truly temperate mixture and the necessity of equality in both quantity and quality of the primary qualities.

Core Idea
True temperance demands perfect balance in both volume and intensity of the four primary qualities.

In this passage, the author defines the truly temperate mixture, explaining that it requires not just equal volumes of the primary qualities but also equal intensity. He then addresses potential objections, using examples like fire and water and boiled vs. icy water to clarify the conditions. This lesson unpacks the core definition and the key conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and context
Start by reading the opening part that reminds you of the four primary qualities and the concept of mixture. Understand that the truly temperate occurs when the qualities are balanced.
Pay attention to the terms 'how many' and 'how strong'.
2
Identify the two requirements
The author states two conditions: equality in quantity (based on volume of loci) and equality in quality (intensity). Note why both are necessary.
The example of boiled vs. icy water shows that equal volume doesn't guarantee equal inclination.
3
Examine the objections and answers
The passage includes several objections. The first argues that fire's heat is much stronger than water's cold. The response uses the counterbalance of water's moisture. The second objection concerns lightness and heaviness, answered by their dependence on heat and cold.
These objections are marginal notes but integral to the discussion.
4
Reflect on the third marginal note
The last objection questions whether equality of qualities alone suffices, using the boiled vs. icy water example. The passage cuts off, but it prompts reflection on the insufficiency of quality alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد علمت أن الكيفيات التي يمكن بينها الفعل والانفعال أربع : الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة وهذه الأربع تسمى بالكيفيات الأول لأن كل واحد من البسائط العنصرية لا يخلو عن اثنين منها كما مر . وهي متضادة فيقع بين كل متضادتين منها كسر وانكسار عند الامتزاج فالمقادير منها الحاصلة في المركب إن كانت متساوية بحسب أحجام محالها متقاومة في أنفسها بحسب الشدة والضعف حتى يحصل منها كيفية عديمة الميل إلى الطرفين فتكون حينئذ على حاق الوسط بينهما فهو المعتدل الحقيقي فقد اعتبر فيه تساوي البسائط كماً وكيفاً وذلك لأن امتناع وجوده كما ذهبوا إليه مبني على تساوي ميول بسائطه ولا بد فيه من تساوي كمياتها لأن الغالب ذلك في الكل الكم يشبه أن يكون غالباً في الميل وليس هذا وحده كافياً في التساوي لأن الميول قد تختلف باختلاف الكيفيات مع الاتحاد في الحجم كما في الماء المغلي بالنار والمبرد بالثلج فإن ميل الثاني بسبب الكثافة والثقل اللازمين من التبريد أشد وأقوى من ميل الأول وربما يكتفي فيه باعتبار تساوي الكيفيات وحدها في قوله: (أما على الأول ) أي الاستحالة فقد ظهر لنا مما ذكر في هذا المقصد أن في المزاج ثلاث مذاهب وأن في كل مذهب قولين. قوله: (تساوي البسائط كماً وكيفاً فإن قيل: لا شك أن حرارة النار في الشدة تكون أضعاف برودة الماء في الشدة. كما تشهد به المشاهدة . قلنا لو سلم ذلك فرطوبة الماء في الشدة تكون أضعاف يبوسة النار في الشدة حتى يطفئ الماء القليل أضعافه من النار كما تشهد به التجربة. فعلى هذا يجب أن يعتبر التساوي بين الماء والنار كماً وكيفاً. فإن قيل : لا بد أن يعتبر التساوي في الخفة والثقل هاهنا أيضاً وهو غير تابع لتساوي الكيفيات الأول، ولا لتساوي الحجم أيضاً قلنا : الذي يلوح من كلامهم هو أن الخفة الطبيعية تابعة للحرارة الطبيعية والثقلة الطبيعية تابعة للبرودة الطبيعية فتأمل. قوله: (وربما يكتفي فيه تساوي الكيفيات فإن قيل: قد مر آنفاً أن حرارة الماء المغلي بالنار تكون في الشدة مساوية لبرودة الماء بالثلج مع أن ميل الثاني أشد وأقوى من ميل الأول.
You have learned that the qualities between which action and passion can occur are four: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. These four are called the primary qualities because each one of the elementary simples is not devoid of two of them, as has passed. They are opposed, so between each pair of opposites a breaking and being broken occurs during mixing. So the quantities of them (i.e., of the four qualities) that occur in the compound, if they are equal according to the sizes of their loci and counterbalancing in themselves according to intensity and weakness, such that a quality without inclination to either extreme results, then at that time it is on the very midpoint between them, and it is the truly temperate. So equality of the simples in quantity and quality is considered in it, because the impossibility of its existence, as they hold, is based on the equality of the inclinations of its simples, and it is necessary to have equality of their quantities because the predominant in the whole—the quantity seems to be predominant in inclination—and this alone is not sufficient for equality, because the inclinations may differ according to the difference of qualities even with unity in volume, as in water boiled by fire and water cooled by snow, for the inclination of the second due to the density and weight necessitated by cooling is stronger and more powerful than the inclination of the first. And perhaps it suffices in it to consider the equality of the qualities alone in... His statement: 'As for the first' i.e., transformation, it has become clear to us from what was mentioned in this aim that there are three views on mixture and that in each view there are two sayings. His statement: 'Equality of the simples in quantity and quality' for if it is said: There is no doubt that the heat of fire in intensity is many times the cold of water in intensity, as observation testifies. We say: Even if that is conceded, the moisture of water in intensity is many times the dryness of fire in intensity, such that a small amount of water extinguishes many times its amount of fire, as experience testifies. On this basis, equality between water and fire must be considered in quantity and quality. If it is said: Equality in lightness and heaviness must also be considered here, and it is not a consequence of equality of the primary qualities nor of equality of volume. We say: What appears from their speech is that natural lightness is consequent upon natural heat, and natural heaviness is consequent upon natural cold, so reflect. His statement: 'And perhaps it suffices in it to consider the equality of the qualities alone' for if it is said: It has just passed that the heat of water boiled by fire is in intensity equal to the cold of water with snow, while the inclination of the second is stronger and more powerful than the inclination of the first.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage defines the truly temperate (al-mu'tadil al-haqiqi) condition in mixtures. The author first reminds that the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) are the basis of interaction. When two opposed qualities mix, they break each other's effect. For a compound to be truly temperate, the quantities of these qualities must be equal in relation to the volumes of their loci and also counterbalanced in intensity. This results in a quality with no inclination to either extreme. The author then explains why both quantity and quality must be considered: quantity alone is not enough because inclinations can vary with quality even at equal volumes. An example is given: boiled water and icy water have equal volumes but different inclinations due to density from cold. The author then includes marginal notes. The first marginal note explains that from this discussion we learn that there are three views on mixture and two sayings in each view. The second marginal note addresses an objection: since fire's heat is much stronger than water's cold, how can they be equal? The response uses the example that water's moisture is much stronger than fire's dryness, so overall there can be equality when both quantity and quality are considered. Then another objection about lightness and heaviness is answered: natural lightness follows heat, and natural heaviness follows cold. The third marginal note raises the objection that if only equality of qualities is considered, the example of boiled water vs. icy water shows that even if heat and cold are equal in intensity, the inclinations differ due to other factors, implying that more than just equality of qualities is needed. The passage cuts off here.

Correction: The author emphasizes that equality in both quantity (volume) and quality (intensity) is necessary, as shown by the example of boiled vs. icy water.

The truly temperate mixture requires equality in both quantity and quality of the four primary qualities to achieve a neutral inclination.

Objection and Answer on True Balanced Temperament
Objection and answer regarding true balanced temperament

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection to the existence of a perfectly balanced temperament and the answer that invokes external causes to explain its possibility.

Core Idea
External causes can hold equal forces together long enough for mixture to occur.

In this passage, the author presents a key objection against the possibility of a truly balanced temperament: if the parts are perfectly equal, they should separate before any mixture can happen. He then provides a nuanced answer, explaining how external factors can force opposite parts together, creating a counterbalance that allows temperament. The source also acknowledges that such a balanced state is rare and unstable, but not impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the reasoning why equal parts cannot mix—they have no inherent reason to stay together and their natural tendencies cause instant separation.
Notice the reliance on principles of force and motion.
2
Identify the key concepts in the objection
Look for terms like 'equal parts', 'mutually resistant', 'separation', and 'action and passion'. Understand how the objector uses these to argue against balanced temperament.
3
Examine the answer
Read the answer that invokes external causes. See how the author admits the rarity but denies impossibility, providing examples of how external forces can keep parts together.
The answer also distinguishes between causes of initial union and causes of continued union.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters for Islamic theology and natural philosophy. The balanced temperament is often invoked in discussions of life and soul.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: وإنه لا يوجد في الخارج إذ أجزاؤه متساوية فلا يقسر بعضها بعضاً على الاجتماع لامتناع أن يغلب بعض من الأمور المتساوية المتقاومة بعضاً آخر منها، وطبائعها داعية إلى الافتراق فيحصل الافتراق قبل حصول الفعل والانفعال فإنه حادث يستدعي مدة معتداً بها لأنه حركة من كيفية إلى أخرى بعيدة عنها بخلاف الافتراق الذي يكفيه أدنى حركة مع كونه موجوداً في كل آن من زمانها فلا يحصل بينها مزاج لتوقفه على حصول تلك الحركة وحدوثه عند انقطاعها. والجواب: أنه ربما تقع الأجزاء لأسباب خارجية بحيث تكون المائلة إلى العلو في جهة السفل والعكس فتتمانع وتتقاوم لتساوي قواها في الميول وتبقى مجتمعة فيحصل المزاج بتفاعلها. نعم يندر وجود ذلك المعتدل ولا يكون باقياً مستمراً إما لسرعة التحلل أو لسرعة غلبة بعض أجزائه على بعض وأما الامتناع فلا، كيف وبقاء الاجتماع قد يكون لمنفصل كأصل الاجتماع الذي لا بد له من مقتض سوى الأجزاء إذ لسبب لبقاء الاجتماع غير منحصر في غلبة عنصر.
They said: And it does not exist in the external world because its parts are equal, so they do not force one another to come together, since it is impossible for one of the equal mutually resistant things to overcome another. Their natures call for separation, so separation occurs before action and passion, which is an event requiring a considerable duration because it is a movement from one quality to another far from it, unlike separation which can occur with minimal movement while it exists in every moment of time. So no temperament occurs between them, since it depends on the occurrence of that movement and its coming into being when it stops. The answer: It is possible that the parts fall, due to external causes, such that those inclined upward are in the downward direction and vice versa. Then they mutually impede and resist due to the equality of their powers in inclinations and remain together, so temperament occurs through their interaction. Yes, the existence of that balanced temperament is rare, and it does not remain permanent, either due to rapid dissolution or rapid overcoming of some of its parts over others. But impossibility is not the case; how can it be, when continued union may be due to a separator, like the original union which must have a cause other than the parts, since the cause for continued union is not limited to the dominance of an element.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the existence of true balanced temperament. The objectors argue that if parts are perfectly equal, they have no reason to stay together—their natural tendencies will cause them to separate before any mixture occurs, because mixture requires a process that takes time, while separation is instantaneous. The answer is that external causes (like mechanical constraints) can force parts of opposite inclinations together. Once forced together, their opposing forces counterbalance, allowing interaction and mixture to take place. The author concedes that such balanced temperament is rare and unstable, but not impossible, because continued union can result from external factors beyond the innate tendencies of the parts.

Misconception: Equal forces can never mix because they always separate before any interaction.
Correction: External constraints can hold equal forces together, allowing mutual resistance and interaction, so mixture is possible albeit rare.

B) By external causes forcing the opposite parts together
C) By the parts losing their natural inclinations

Key Takeaway
External causes can force equal parts to interact, making a balanced temperament possible, though rare and unstable.
