Response to al-Razi and Seven Terrestrial Causes Affecting Temperature
The author responds to al-Razi's argument and lists seven types of terrestrial causes affecting heat and cold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi's argument about climate and temperament is refuted through a detailed enumeration of seven terrestrial causes that modify heat and cold.

Core Idea
Seven earthly factors, not just latitude, determine a region's temperature and climate.

Al-Razi argued that certain lands' intense heat indicates their inhabitants' temperament tilts toward heat, challenging the idea of a single balanced clime. The author responds by showing that observed heat can be due to various local conditions. This passage details seven categories of terrestrial causes that affect heating and cooling, explaining why climate varies beyond simple latitudinal expectations.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to follow the argument from al-Razi's objection to the enumeration of causes.
The passage begins with a rebuttal, then al-Razi's new argument, then the author's response.
2
Understand the initial exchange
Identify the first response (qulna) and al-Razi's argument about lands with double total declination. Note how the author counters.
The author's initial answer is rejected as weak; al-Razi's argument is based on observed blackness and hair of inhabitants.
3
Analyze the author's answer
See that the author attributes heat to long days and to terrestrial positions. Then focus on the seven causes listed.
The seven causes are the core of the response, showing multiple factors.
4
Examine each terrestrial cause
Go through each of the seven causes: low/high land, mountains, sea, soil types, winds, vegetation, and celestial positions. Understand how each affects temperature.
The seventh cause is cut off; note the reference to 'Kulliyat al-Qanun'.
5
Connect to the broader topic
Reflect on how this enumeration defends the concept of true moderation against al-Razi's critique.
The discussion shows that climate is complex and not a simple indicator of fundamental temperament.

قلنا : ما ذكرته تابع للاعتدال بمعنى آخر هو الاعتدال الطبي لا الاعتدال الحقيقي الذي كلامنا فيه. وليس هذا الجواب بشيء لأن مزاج الإنسان كما مر أقرب إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي فإذا كان مزاج هؤلاء أكبر توفراً لما ينبغي للمزاج الإنساني كان أقرب إليه وأعدل لا محالة. ثم قال الإمام: إنا نرى بلاداً أعرضها بقدر الميل الكلي مرتين يكون صيفهم كشتاء خط الاستواء في بعد الشمس عن سمت الرأس ثم صيفهم في غاية الحر فكذا شتاء خط الاستواء يكون في غاية الحر. فما ظنك بصيفهم وشدة حره؟ فيكون مزاجهم مائلاً إلى الحرارة، ويدل عليه شدة سواد سكانها من أهل الزنج والحبشة وشدة جعودة شعورهم. والجواب : إن ذلك الحر في صيف تلك البلاد قد يكون بسبب طول نهارهم ومكث الشمس فوق أفقهم كثيراً وقد يكون بواسطة أوضاع وأحوال أرضية فإنها تؤثر في التسخين والتبريد بأنواع: الأول : المنخفض من الأرض أحر من المرتفع لانعكاس الأشعة وقلة هبوب الرياح فيه بخلاف المرتفع. الثاني : الجبل المجاور للبلد قد يعين الشعاع بعكسه كما إذا كان في المغرب أو في أحد جانبي الشمال والجنوب وقد يمنعه كما إذا كان في جانب المشرق، وقد يعكس الجبل الريح وقد يمنه فيختلف بذلك حال الحر والبرد. الثالث : البحر فإن مجاورته ترطب قطعا ثم قد يسخن البحر بصقالته وانعكاس الأشعة منه وقد يبرد إذا كان شمالياً إذ قد يكتسب الشمال منه برداً. الرابع: النزية والسبخة والكبريتية والزاجية تسخن، والصخرية والرملية تحفظ الحر والبرد. الخامس : الرياح فالشمال تبرد لمرورها على بلاد باردة فيها ثلوج ومياه منجمدة وتجفف أيضا ليبوستها إذ لا تمر بالمياه لأن أكثر البحور في جانب الجنوب لا تخالطها الأبخرة الكثيرة والجنوب تسخن وترطب بعكس ما مر. والقبول والدبور بين. السادس : مجاورة الآجام والأشجار والمباقل وغيرها من المعادن تؤثر في الهواء تأثيرا يناسبها. السابع الأوضاع الواقعة في طالع البقعة من اجتماع كواكب فيه تقتضى سخونتها أو برودتها والأوضاع الحادثة في كل وقت بالقياس إلى تلك البقعة كمرور بعض الكواكب بسمت رأسها. وذكر في كليات القانون أن من التغيرات التابعة للأمور السماوية مثل أن يجتمع كثير من الدراري في جزء واحد من
We said: What you mentioned is consequent to moderation in another sense – it is medical moderation, not true moderation which is our topic. But this answer is nothing, because the temperament of man, as previously mentioned, is closer to true moderation, and if the temperament of these people has a greater abundance of what is appropriate for human temperament, then it is necessarily closer and more balanced. Then the Imam said: We see a land whose latitude is twice the total declination, their summer is like the winter of the equator in terms of the sun's distance from the zenith, then their summer is extremely hot, so likewise the winter of the equator is extremely hot. What do you think of their summer and its intensity? Their temperament inclines to heat, and this is indicated by the intense blackness of its inhabitants – the Zanj and Abyssinians – and the intense curliness of their hair. The answer: That heat in the summer of those lands may be due to the length of their days and the sun's long remaining above their horizon, and it may be due to terrestrial positions and conditions, for they affect heating and cooling in various types: First: Low land is hotter than high land due to reflection of rays and lack of wind, unlike high land. Second: A mountain adjacent to a region may aid radiation by reflecting it, as if it is in the west or on one side of north and south, and it may block it as if it is in the east, and the mountain may reflect wind or block it, thus the condition of heat and cold differs. Third: The sea: its vicinity certainly humidifies, then the sea may heat up due to its smoothness and reflection of rays from it, and may cool down if it is northern, for the north wind may acquire cold from it. Fourth: Marshy, saline, sulfuric, and vitriolic soils heat up; rocky and sandy soils retain heat and cold. Fifth: Winds: the north wind cools because it passes over cold lands with snow and frozen waters and also dries due to its dryness, as it does not pass over waters because most seas in the southern side are not mixed with many vapors; the south wind heats and humidifies opposite to the above; the east and west winds are intermediate. Sixth: Proximity to thickets, trees, vegetable gardens, and other minerals affects the air in a way suited to them. Seventh: The positions occurring in the ascendant of the locality – like the conjunction of planets in it requiring its heat or cold – and the positions occurring at each time relative to that locality, like the passage of some planets over its zenith. And it is mentioned in the 'Kulliyat al-Qanun' that among the changes dependent on celestial matters, such as the gathering of many bright stars in one part of
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a sustained objection-and-answer exchange. First, the author presents a response (qulna) to al-Razi's earlier claim that the fourth clime is most balanced, arguing that his evidence points only to medical moderation, not true moderation. However, the author immediately concedes this reply is weak because the human temperament is innately closest to true moderation. Al-Razi then advances a new argument: lands at twice the total declination have summers like equator winters but then extremely hot summers, implying their temperament tilts to heat, shown by the blackness and hair of their peoples. The answer to this is detailed: such heat may be due to long days or, more importantly, to various terrestrial factors. The author then enumerates seven types of causes affecting temperature: 1) low vs. high land, 2) adjacent mountains, 3) sea proximity, 4) soil types (marshy, saline, etc.), 5) winds (north, south, east, west), 6) vegetation, and 7) celestial positions (planetary conjunctions, transits). This enumeration explains why observed heat does not necessarily indicate a fundamental lack of moderation in temperament, but rather local modifications.

Refutation of al-Razi's Argument on Heat and Temperament
The author refutes al-Razi's claim by enumerating seven terrestrial causes of temperature variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi's inference from the heat of certain regions to the winter of the equator is invalid, and how seven terrestrial causes explain temperature variations independently of solar altitude.

Core Idea
Heat depends on many causes, not just the sun; al-Razi's argument fails because it ignores them.

Imam al-Razi objected to Ibn Sina's claim about balanced temperament, arguing that certain regions have extremely hot summers, implying the equator's winter is also hot. The author responds by showing that terrestrial conditions—altitude, mountains, seas, soil, winds, vegetation, and celestial positions—can independently cause heat or cold. This lesson examines al-Razi's objection and the author's comprehensive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection carefully
Follow the argument: a country with 2x inclination, its summer like equator's winter, then its summer very hot, so equator's winter must be very hot. Note the conclusion about temperament and skin color.
2
Identify the author's initial response
Notice that the author does not deny the heat but offers alternative explanations: length of day and terrestrial conditions. Then he enumerates seven types.
3
Study the seven causes
Go through each of the seven types: low/high land, mountains, sea, soil types, winds, vegetation, celestial positions. Understand how each can affect temperature.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since these causes are plausible, al-Razi's inference is invalid. Moreover, reason allows a more balanced temperament elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الإمام: (إنا نرى بلاداً أعرضها بقدر الميل الكلي مرتين يكون صيفهم كشتاء خط الاستواء في بعد الشمس عن سمت الرأس ( ثم صيفهم في غاية الحر فكذا شتاء خط الاستواء ) يكون في غاية الحر. ( فما ظنك بصيفهم وشدة حره ؟ فيكون مزاجهم مائلاً إلى الحرارة، ويدل عليه شدة سواد سكانها من أهل الزنج والحبشة وشدة جعودة شعورهم ) والجواب : إن ذلك ) الحر في صيف تلك البلاد قد يكون بسبب طول نهارهم ومكث الشمس فوق أفقهم كثيراً و (قد يكون بواسطة أوضاع وأحوال أرضية فإنها تؤثر) في التسخين والتبريد ( بأنواع: الأول : المنخفض) من الأرض (أحر) من المرتفع ) لانعكاس الأشعة وقلة هبوب الرياح ( فيه ) بخلاف المرتفع ( الثاني : الجبل ) المجاور للبلد ( قد يعين الشعاع بعكسه ) كما إذا كان في المغرب أو في أحد جانبي الشمال والجنوب ( وقد يمنعه ) كما إذا كان في جانب المشرق ، ( وقد يعكس) الجبل ( الريح وقد يمنه) فيختلف بذلك حال الحر والبرد الثالث : البحر فإن مجاورته ترطب ( قطعا ( ثم قد يسخن البحر بصقالته وانعكاس الأشعة ( منه ( وقد يبرد إذا كان شمالياً إذ قد يكتسب الشمال منه برداً. الرابع: النزية والسبخة والكبريتية والزاجية تسخن، والصخرية والرملية تحفظ الحر والبرد الخامس : الرياح فالشمال تبرد لمرورها على بلاد باردة فيها ثلوج ومياه منجمدة وتجفف أيضا ليبوستها إذ لا تمر بالمياه لأن أكثر البحور في جانب الجنوب لا تخالطها الأبخرة الكثيرة ) والجنوب تسخن وترطب بعكس ما مر . (والقبول والدبور بين السادس : مجاورة الآجام والأشجار والمباقل وغيرها ) من المعادن ( تؤثر) في الهواء تأثيرا يناسبها ( السابع الأوضاع الواقعة في طالع البقعة ) من اجتماع كواكب فيه تقتضى سخونتها أو برودتها (و) الأوضاع الحادثة في كل وقت ) بالقياس إلى تلك البقعة كمرور بعض الكواكب بسمت رأسها . وذكر في كليات القانون أن من التغيرات التابعة للأمور السماوية مثل أن يجتمع كثير من الدراري في جزء واحد من قوله : (قال الإمام الرازي أي قال معارضاً لابن سينا فيما ذكره آنفاً. وقوله: بعكسه أي بعكس الجبل الشعاع فالمصدر مضاف إلى الفاعل والمفعول متروك، ويحتمل أن يكون الضمير المجرور راجعاً إلى الشعاع فالمصدر مضاف إلى المفعول، والفاعل متروك . وقوله : الدراري الدر هو الثاقب المضيء . وقوله : ما هو مفعول يوجب ( وإذا كان ذلك ) الذي ذكرناه ) محتملا بطل الاستدلال ) لجواز أن يكون الحر في صيف تلك البلاد لبعض هذه الأسباب لا لمجرد قرب الشمس من سمت رؤوسها فلا يلزم أن يكون شتاء خط الاستواء مثله في الحرارة إذا كان خالياً عن الأسباب المذكورة. (ثم لا مانع من جهة العقل ( أن يوجب ) في بعض المواضع التي ليس من خط الاستواء ولا من الإقليم الرابع ) بعض هذه الأمور ) أي في بعض الأوضاع الأرضية (إما مفردة أو مركبة ما هو ( أي مزاجاً صنفياً هو (أعدل من (الاثنين) أي مزاجي سكان الاستواء والإقليم الرابع
Then the Imam said: 'We see a country whose width is twice the total inclination; their summer is like the winter of the equator in terms of the sun's distance from the zenith, then their summer is extremely hot, so likewise the winter of the equator is extremely hot. What do you think of their summer and its intensity? So their temperament inclines toward heat, and this is indicated by the intense blackness of its inhabitants from the Zanj and Abyssinia and the intense curliness of their hair.' The answer: That heat in the summer of those countries may be due to the length of their days and the sun's long stay above their horizon, and it may be due to terrestrial conditions and states that affect heating and cooling in types: First: low land is hotter than high land due to reflection of rays and lack of wind, unlike high land. Second: the mountain adjacent to the country may aid the rays by reflection, as when it is in the west or on one side of north and south, or may block them, as when it is in the east, and the mountain may reflect the wind or block it, so the state of heat and cold varies accordingly. Third: the sea; its vicinity moistens absolutely, then the sea may heat up due to its smoothness and reflection of rays from it, or it may cool if it is northern, since it may acquire cold from the north. Fourth: nitrous, saline, sulphurous, and aluminous soils heat, while rocky and sandy soils preserve heat and cold. Fifth: winds; the north wind cools because it passes over cold lands with snow and frozen waters, and also dries due to its dryness since it does not pass over waters because most seas are on the south side, and it does not mix with many vapors; the south wind heats and moistens opposite to what preceded; the east and west winds are between. Sixth: vicinity of thickets, trees, vegetable plots, and other minerals, which affect the air appropriately. Seventh: the positions occurring in the ascendant of the locality, from conjunctions of planets that necessitate its heat or cold, and the positions occurring at every time relative to that locality, like the passage of some planets through its zenith. And it was mentioned in the Canon of Medicine that among the changes dependent on celestial matters is that many stars gather in one part... His saying: 'The Imam al-Razi said, i.e., he said objecting to Ibn Sina regarding what he mentioned earlier. And his saying: 'by its opposite' i.e., the mountain reflects the rays, so the verbal noun is annexed to the agent and the object is omitted; it may be that the annexed pronoun refers to the rays, so the verbal noun is annexed to the object and the agent is omitted. And his saying: 'al-darari' – al-durr is the penetrating bright. And his saying: 'what is' is a direct object requiring... And if that which we mentioned is probable, then the inference is invalid, because it is possible that the heat in the summer of those countries is due to some of these causes, not merely to the proximity of the sun to their zenith; so it does not necessarily follow that the winter of the equator is similar in heat if it lacks the mentioned causes. Then there is no rational impediment that some of these things, either singly or combined, cause in some places that are neither on the equator nor in the fourth clime what is—i.e., a specific temperament that is more balanced than the two, i.e., the temperaments of the inhabitants of the equator and the fourth clime.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection by Imam al-Razi concerning the relationship between latitude, heat, and human temperament. The author first presents al-Razi's argument: that certain countries with a specific width have summers as hot as the equator's winter, implying that the equator's winter is extremely hot and thus its inhabitants' temperament is excessively hot, as seen in their black skin and curly hair. The author then provides a detailed answer, enumerating seven types of terrestrial conditions that affect heating and cooling, thereby showing that heat in such regions may be due to these factors rather than mere solar proximity. The seven causes are: (1) low altitude vs. high altitude; (2) mountains reflecting or blocking rays; (3) seas and their temperature; (4) soil types; (5) winds; (6) vegetation and minerals; (7) celestial positions. The author also references the Canon of Medicine regarding celestial influences. After this enumeration, the author concludes that since these alternative causes are possible, al-Razi's inference is invalid. Furthermore, the author states that reason does not preclude the existence of a place, not on the equator or in the fourth clime, where a more balanced temperament may occur due to a combination of these factors. The passage then includes a brief grammatical gloss on certain phrases from the original text.

First Objection: Digestive Power as Sole Mover
First objection against distinguishing digestive and nutritive powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that identifies the digestive power as the sole mover, thereby conflating it with the nutritive power.

Core Idea
The digestive power alone suffices as mover, eliminating the need for a separate nutritive power.

This passage introduces a key objection against distinguishing the digestive and nutritive powers. The argument, drawing on Ibn Sina's principle, claims that the very power which moves food from its original form to the organic form is both the remover and the bringer. Thus, the digestive power is sufficient to perform both roles, making the nutritive power redundant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Begin by reading the opening lines where the objection is introduced. Notice that it claims the mover to similarity is the same as the power that brings to the organ.
The objection is attributed to 'the Imam' and refers to earlier commentators.
2
Analyze the syllogism
Identify the minor premise (digestive power moves food to organic form) and the major premise (whatever moves something to a goal is the bringer). See how they lead to the conclusion that one power suffices.
The syllogism is explicit in the text.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's support
Read Ibn Sina's principle that between two motions there is a rest, and the cause of removal must be the same as the cause of arrival. Understand how it reinforces the objection.
This principle is cited to rule out two separate powers.
4
Conclude the objection
Trace how the argument ends with the claim that the digestive power is the nutritive power itself. Reflect on the implication for the classification of internal powers.
The conclusion directly challenges the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعترض الإمام عليه أولا بما أشار إليه المنصف بقوله : ( ويمكن أن يقال : المحرك إلى مشابه العضو هو القوة الموصلة إليه ) . وتقريره على ما في المباحث المشرقية أن القوة الهاضمة محركة للغذاء في الكيف إلى الصورة المشابهة لصورة العضو وكل ما حرك شيئاً إلى شيء آخر فهو الموصل إلى ذلك الآخر، فيكون الفاعل للفعلين قوة واحدة . أما الصغرى فظاهرة إذ لا معنى للهضم إلا التحريك عن الصورة الغذائية إلى الصورة العضوية وأما الكبرى فظاهرة أيضاً لأن ما حرك شيئاً إلى شيء كان المتوجه إليه غاية للمحرك والمعنى بكونه غاية أن المقصود الأصلي هو فعل ذلك الشيء . وقد اعترف ابن سينا بذلك حيث احتج على أن بين كل حركتين سكوناً ، فقال : محال أن يكون الواصل إلى حد ما واصلاً إليه بلا علة موجودة موصلة . ومحال أن تكون هذه العلة غير التي أزالت عن المستقر الأول، هذا كلامه وهو يقتضي أنه لما كان المزيل عن الصورة الدموية هو الهاضمة وجب أن يكون الموصل إلى العضوية أيضاً الهاضمة، فهي الغاذية لا غير
Then the Imam objected to him first by what the fair commentator indicated by his statement: 'It can be said: The mover to similarity with the organ is the power that brings it to that organ.' And its explanation according to what is in the Eastern Investigations is that the digestive power moves the food in quality to the form similar to the form of the organ, and everything that moves something to something else is the bringer to that other; thus the agent of the two actions is one power. As for the minor premise, it is apparent, for there is no meaning of digestion except moving from the nutritive form to the organic form. And as for the major premise, it is also apparent, because whatever moves something to something, the thing toward which it moves is a goal for the mover, and the meaning of its being a goal is that the primary intention is the actualization of that thing. And Ibn Sina acknowledged this when he argued that between every two motions there is a rest, saying: It is impossible for something reaching a limit to reach it without an existing cause that brings it. And it is impossible for this cause to be other than the one that removed it from the first starting point. This is his statement, and it implies that since the remover from the blood form is the digestive power, then the bringer to the organic form must also be the digestive power, so it is the nutritive power, not otherwise.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against distinguishing the digestive and nutritive powers. The objection argues that the digestive power itself is the mover that brings food to similarity with the organ, so it should also be considered the nutritive power, making the two powers one. The argument is structured as a syllogism: the digestive power moves food to the organic form (minor premise), and anything that moves something to a goal is the bringer to that goal (major premise), so the digestive power is also the nutritive power. The objection supports this with Ibn Sina's principle that between every two motions there is a rest, and the cause that removes from an initial state is the same that brings to the final state. Therefore, the digestive power alone suffices and is identical to the nutritive power.

Key Terms
الهاضمة
الغاذية
المحرك
الموصل
الصورة
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies any distinction between digestive and nutritive powers.
Correction: The objection does not deny a conceptual distinction, but argues that the digestive power alone is sufficient to perform both functions, making a separate nutritive power unnecessary.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what principle does Ibn Sina use to argue that the mover to an end is the same as the remover from the beginning?
A) The principle of non-contradiction
B) The principle that between every two motions there is a rest
C) The principle of sufficient reason
D) The principle of causality

Key Takeaway
The first objection contends that the digestive power alone suffices because it moves food to the organic form, and thus it is identical to the nutritive power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This objection is structurally sound, yet the lesson disagrees with it. When you encounter a well-argued objection to your beliefs, do you honestly examine its premises, or do you reflexively defend your position? What would it take for you to change your mind?
?
Think of an area in your life where you have collapsed multiple responsibilities into one, assuming one cause suffices. Could this be a form of neglect? How does this passage challenge you to reexamine the roles you play?

Second Objection: Digestive Power's Preparation Suffices for Form Emanation
Presents the second objection against distinguishing digestive and nutritive powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the distinction between digestive and nutritive powers, which argues that the digestive power's preparation alone suffices for form emanation from the Bestower of forms.

Core Idea
Digestive preparation alone suffices; no separate nutritive power is needed.

This lesson examines a nuanced objection to the distinction between digestive and nutritive powers. The objection leverages the concept of the Bestower of forms (Wahib al-Suwar) to argue that once the digestive power adequately prepares the matter, the form emanates, making a separate nutritive power redundant. Let's read the passage to see the argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the argument's structure: it begins with a rhetorical 'How?' and proceeds to describe the roles of the preparer, emanator, and digestive power.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify key terms and their roles
Identify 'الهاضمة' (digestive power), 'الغاذية' (nutritive power), 'واهب الصور' (Bestower of forms). Understand that the objection claims that the digestive power's cooking and maturing produce the necessary preparation (الاستعداد) for the form to emanate.
The term 'المفيض' (emanator) refers to the Bestower of forms.
3
Analyze the logical argument
The objection states: when preparation is complete and preparedness perfected, the form emanates and nutrition is completed. Therefore, the digestive power alone suffices, and there is no need for a separate nutritive power. Ask yourself: why does the objector think this follows?
The objector is using the concept of the Bestower of forms to collapse two powers into one.
4
Reflect on the wider context
Consider that this objection is part of a larger debate about whether different bodily powers are distinct or reducible. This argument challenges the necessity of a nutritive power separate from the digestive power.
This connects to broader discussions of causality and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض ثانياً بما ذكره المصنف بقوله: ( كيف والمراد بالقوة هنا المعدة ( للمادة لفيضان الصورة عليها ( والمفيض ) لها وهو ( واهب الصور و القوة (الهاضمة هي المفيدة بطبخها ونضجها ( للاستعدادات المختلفة بالقوة ) أي الشدة ( والضعف التي من جملتها ما يعد ) المادة (لفيضان الصورة العضوية وتلك القوة المفيدة لهذه الاستعدادات ) مغنية عن قوى أخرى في الأعضاء ) لأنه إذا تم الإعداد وكمل الاستعداد فاضت الصورة وتمت التغذية، فإذن لا فرق بين الهاضمة ،والغاذية
And he objected secondly by what the author mentioned in his statement: 'How? What is meant by the power here is the preparer of matter for the emanation of form upon it, and the emanator of it is the Bestower of forms. The digestive power is the one that provides, by its cooking and maturing, the different preparations in potentiality (i.e. intensity and weakness), among which is what prepares matter for the emanation of the organic form. And that power which provides these preparations suffices for other powers in the organs, because when the preparation is complete and the preparedness is perfected, the form emanates and nutrition is completed. So then there is no difference between the digestive and the nutritive power.'
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against distinguishing the digestive and nutritive powers. The objection argues that the digestive power prepares the matter to receive the form from the Bestower of forms. Once preparation is complete, the form emanates and nutrition is accomplished. Therefore, the digestive power alone is sufficient, and there is no need for a separate nutritive power. The objection uses the concept of the Bestower of forms (واهب الصور) and emphasizes that the digestive power's cooking and maturing produce various preparations that culminate in the organic form. Thus, the two powers are effectively one.

Key Terms

الغاذية
المعدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection denies the emanation of forms from the Bestower of forms.
Correction: The objection actually relies on the Bestower of forms to argue that the digestive power's preparation alone suffices; it does not deny the Bestower's role.

Checkpoint

A) It directly gives the organic form
B) It prepares the food for digestion
C) It moves the food in the body
D) It retains food in the stomach

Key Takeaway
The second objection argues that the digestive power's preparation alone suffices for form emanation, rendering a separate nutritive power unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own preparations—like studying, working, or planning—do you genuinely acknowledge that the outcome comes from Allah, the Bestower? Or do you feel that your effort alone guarantees the result? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that your preparations are only a means and the real emanation is from Him?
?
The objection says the digestive power's preparation suffices, but only because the form emanates from Allah. In your life, what is a 'preparation' you have been relying on as if it were the cause, rather than seeing it as a mere means from Allah? How can you shift your heart's attachment from the means to the Bestower?
?
The objector argued no separate nutritive power is needed—the digestive power alone is enough because Allah's act completes it. Are there areas where you have invented 'separate powers'—extra efforts, excuses, or justifications—because you doubt that Allah's provision through simple means is sufficient? What would it look like to be content with the means He has given you and trust His completion?

Objection: Can Simple Substances Be Food?
Questions the medical consensus that simple substances cannot be food for animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the medical view that simple substances cannot be food for animals and the analogy from plants.

Core Idea
Plants thrive on simple substances like water, so why can't animals?

Medical experts often claim that simple substances such as water cannot serve as food for animals. However, this claim lacks proof and appears contradicted by the behavior of plants. In this lesson, we examine the reasoning behind this objection and consider how it challenges the standard view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice the author's tone: he states a common view, then immediately questions it.
The key phrase is 'فلم لا يجوز مثله في الحيوان؟' — a rhetorical question asking why animals can't do the same.
2
Identify the medical consensus
The passage begins with 'المشهور فيما بين الأطباء أن البسيط لا غذاء للحيوان'. This is the claim being challenged.
Simple substances are things like water, salt, etc.
3
Analyze the objection
The author says there is no proof ('ولا برهان عليه') and points out a contradiction: plants absorb water and make it part of their bodies. So why can't animals?
Consider the logical force: if plants can do it, then the medical claim is not universal.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The final question 'فلم لا يجوز مثله في الحيوان?' invites the reader to see the inconsistency. Think about whether you agree or if there might be a hidden assumption.
This type of objection is common in theological and philosophical debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشهور فيما بين الأطباء أن البسيط لا غذاء للحيوان ولا برهان عليه بل فيه إشكال إذ لا شك أن النبات يجذب الماء ويصير ذلك الماء جزءا منه فلم لا يجوز مثله في الحيوان؟
The famous view among physicians is that a simple substance is not food for animals, but there is no proof for it; rather, there is a problem, for there is no doubt that the plant attracts water and that water becomes a part of it, so why is the like not permissible in the animal?
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the widely held medical opinion that simple substances (like water) cannot serve as food for animals. The author argues that there is no proof for this view, and in fact it is problematic because plants do exactly that—they absorb water and incorporate it into their structure. The rhetorical question challenges the reader to explain why animals could not similarly derive nourishment from simple substances.

Key Terms
البسيط
غذاء
الحيوان
النبات
الماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple substances like water cannot be food for animals because they are indigestible.
Correction: The passage points out that plants absorb water and become part of them, showing that simple substances can indeed be incorporated into a living being. This suggests the same may be possible for animals.

Checkpoint

A) Animals can digest water according to experiments
B) Plants absorb water and make it part of themselves
C) Simple substances are found in animal tissues
D) There is a consensus among philosophers

Key Takeaway
The passage challenges the medical dogma that simple substances are not food for animals by using the analogy of plants absorbing water.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author questions a medical consensus with a simple analogy. Is there a truth you have been ignoring because it seems too simple? Will you dare to ask 'why not' like this passage does?

Galen's Objection to Imprinting Theory and Its Refutation
Refutes Galen's objection by distinguishing between image and object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the imprinting theory of vision is defended against Galen's objection by distinguishing between the image in the eye and the external object.

Core Idea
The image in the eye is not the object itself; it is merely a condition for seeing the external object.

Galen raised a simple but powerful objection: if the eye is small, how can it contain the image of the entire world? The answer lies in a critical distinction between the image (shabah) and the actual object. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how it saves the imprinting theory.

Guided Path
1
Understand Galen's objection
Consider the problem: the eye is small, yet we see a vast world. Galen argued this is impossible if the image of the world must physically fit in the eye.
The objection hinges on the assumption that the image must be the same size as the object.
2
Analyze the answer
The author responds that the image of a large object can exist in a small space; only the actual object itself cannot fit. This is not physically impossible.
Focus on the Arabic phrase: 'لا يمتنع حصول شبح الكبير في الصغير'.
3
Recognize the key distinction
The correct view is that the image in the eye is a condition for sight, not the object of sight. The object of sight is the external thing itself. This distinction neutralizes Galen's objection.
The author endorses this as 'الحق' (the truth) on the theory of imprinting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأشياء ( إلا قدر نقطة الناظر منها ) وهو السواد الأصغر الذي فيه إنسان العين ( لكنا نبصر نصف كرة العالم والجواب : إنه لا يمتنع حصول شبح الكبير في الصغير إنما . المحال حصول ذلك الشكل ( الكبير ( بعينه ) في الصغير ( والحاصل ) مما ذكرنا في الجواب أن هذا الذي أورده جالينوس ( إنما يرد على من يرى) ويعتقد أن المبصر نفس الشبح ) المنطبع في الجليدية كما توهمه المتأخرون من كلام المعلم الأول وحكوه عنه ( وأما من يزعم أن حصول الشبح شرط للإبصار ) وأن المبصر هو ذلك الأمر الخارجي ( فلا يرد عليه ذلك ) الذي أورده فإن شبح الشيء قد لا يساويه في المقدار وإن كان موجباً لإبصاره على ما هو عليه ( وهذا) الأخير ( هو الحق) على القول بالانطباع وفي الملخص: إن المتأخرين لم يفهموا كلامه فحكوه على ما لا ينبغي، فتارة قالوا إن هذه الصورة نفس الإبصار وأخرى قالوا: إنها الإبصار والمبصر معاً، وأما الموجود الخارجي فغير مرئي أصلاً، ثم إنهم تعصبوا لهذه الخرافات وعرضوا معلمهم لطعن الطاعنين فهم كالرواة السوء للشاعر الجيد.
Things (except the size of the point of the eye) which is the smallest blackness in which the pupil of the eye is (but we see half the sphere of the world. The answer: it is not impossible for the image of the large to exist in the small; indeed impossible is the existence of that (large) shape (itself) in the small. (The conclusion) from what we mentioned in the answer is that this which Galen brought forward (only applies to one who sees) and believes that the object of sight is the very image) imprinted in the crystalline humor as the later scholars understood from the words of the First Teacher and reported from him. (But as for one who claims that the existence of the image is a condition for sight) and that the object of sight is that external thing, (then that) which he brought forward does not apply to him), for the image of a thing may not equal it in magnitude, even if it causes one to see it as it is. (And this) last (is the truth) according to the theory of imprinting. And in al-Mulakhkhas: The later scholars did not understand his words, so they reported them improperly; sometimes they said this form is sight itself, and other times they said it is both sight and the object of sight, while the external existent is not visible at all. Then they became fanatical for these superstitions and exposed their teacher to the criticism of critics; they are like evil narrators of a good poet.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion. It begins by stating an objection from Galen: if the eye sees the entire hemisphere of the world, but only a small image fits in the eye, how can the large be seen? The author responds that it is not impossible for the image of a large object to exist in a small space; what is impossible is for the large shape itself to fit in the small. The key is that the image (shabah) in the eye is not identical to the external object; it is only a representation. Galen's objection applies only to those who hold that the object of sight is the very image imprinted in the eye (as some later scholars misunderstood from Aristotle). But if one holds that the image is merely a condition for sight, and that the actual object of sight is the external thing, then Galen's objection does not apply. The author endorses this latter view as correct according to the theory of imprinting. The passage then criticizes later scholars for misrepresenting Aristotle's position, attributing to him the view that the image is sight itself, or both sight and object, and that the external thing is invisible. This is presented as a misunderstanding and misrepresentation.

Key Terms
الشبح (al-shabah: image)
الجليدية (al-jalidiyya: crystalline humor)
المبصر (al-mubsir: object of sight)
الانطباع (al-intiba': imprinting)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the entire external object must physically enter the eye for vision to occur.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only an image (shabah) is necessary; the external object itself does not enter the eye.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct understanding of the imprinting theory that avoids Galen's objection?
A) The external object itself enters the eye.
B) The image in the eye is identical to the object of sight.
C) The image in the eye is a condition for sight, but the object of sight is the external thing.
D) The eye emits a ray that meets the object.

Key Takeaway
The correct understanding of imprinting is that the image in the eye is a condition for sight, not the object itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The later scholars became fanatical for their misunderstandings and exposed their teacher to criticism. Which scholar or teacher do you follow, and how certain are you that you understand them correctly? What specific step will you take today to verify a claim you have heard from them?
?
Galen’s objection seemed logical but rested on a false assumption. What assumption about your faith or practice might you be holding that is similarly flawed? How can you test it against the original sources (Qur’an and Sunnah) rather than relying on hearsay?
?
The author distinguishes between the image (shabah) and the true object (mubsir). In your worship, are you sometimes focused on the ‘image’—the outward forms and rituals—while losing sight of the true object: sincere devotion to Allah? How can you refocus on the essence today?

The Three Modes of Ray Emission
Enumerates three sub-modes of the emission theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three variations of the emission theory of vision as proposed by mathematicians.

Core Idea
The emission theory explains vision by a cone of rays from the eye.

The second theory of vision states that a radiative body exits the eye in the form of a cone. Mathematicians disagreed on its exact nature, giving three distinct models: a solid cone, a bundle of thin lines, or a single scanning ray. This lesson unpacks each model and how they account for vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three modes
Read the Arabic description of the three modes carefully. Note how each mode conceptualizes the ray structure differently.
The passage uses technical terms like 'mukhruṭ' (cone) and 'khuṭūṭ mustaqīma' (straight lines).
2
Identify the first mode: solid cone
The first mode says the cone is solid (muṣmat). Consider: how would a solid cone account for seeing?
3
Identify the second mode: bundle of lines
The second mode says the cone is made of many thin lines. Note the explanation of why tiny objects are invisible.
This mode explains why very small parts are hidden.
4
Identify the third mode: scanning ray
The third mode says a single thin ray exits and moves rapidly over the object. Think about how this differs from the others.
This mode emphasizes motion over structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثاني : إنه يخرج من العين جسم شعاعي على هيئة مخروط متحقق (رأسه يلي العين وقاعدته تلي المبصر والإدراك التام إنما يحصل من الموضع الذي هو موضع المخروط) وهو مذهب جمهور الرياضين، ثم إنهم اختلفوا فيه على وجوه ثلاثة : الأول : إن ذلك المخروط مصمت. الثاني : إنه ملتئم من خطوط مستقيمة شعاعية هي أجسام دقاق قد اجتمع أطرافها عند مركز البصر وامتدت متفرقة إلى المبصر فما وقع عليه أطراف تلك الخطوط أدركه البصر وما وقع بين أطرافها لم يدركه، ولذلك يخفى على البصر الأجزاء التي في غاية الصغر الثالث : إنه يخرج من العين جسم شعاعي رفيع كأنه خط واحد مستقيم ينتهي إلى المبصر ثم يتحرك على سطحه حركة سريعة جدا في طول المرئي وعرضه فيحصل الإدراك به. واحتجوا على مذهبهم بأن الإنسان إذا رأى وجهه في المرآة فليس ذلك لانطباع صورته فيها وإلا كانت منطبعة في موضع معين منها ولم تختلف باختلاف أمكنة الرائي من الجوانب بل لأن الشعاع خرج من العين إلى المرآة ثم انعكس منها لصقالتها إلى الوجه. ألا يرى أنه إذا قرب الوجه منها تخيل أن صورته مرتسمة في سطحها وإذا بعد عنها توهم أنها غائرة فيها مع علمنا بأن المرآة ليس لها غور بذلك المقدار.
The second opinion: that a radiative body exits the eye in the form of a realized cone (its vertex next to the eye and its base next to the object of sight, and complete perception only occurs from the place that is the location of the cone). This is the doctrine of the majority of mathematicians. Then they differed regarding it in three ways: First: that cone is solid. Second: it is composed of straight radiative lines which are fine bodies, their ends meeting at the center of vision and spreading out separately to the object of sight; whatever falls upon the ends of those lines the eye perceives, and whatever falls between their ends it does not perceive; therefore, extremely small parts are hidden from the eye. Third: a thin radiative body exits the eye, as if it were a single straight line, reaching the object of sight, then it moves over its surface very quickly in length and width of the visible object, and thus perception occurs. They argued for their doctrine by the fact that when a person sees his face in a mirror, that is not due to the imprint of his image in it, otherwise it would be imprinted in a specific place in it and would not differ according to the varying positions of the viewer from the sides; rather, because a ray exited from the eye to the mirror and then reflected from it due to its polish to the face. Do you not see that when he brings his face near it, he imagines his image traced on its surface, and when he moves away from it, he imagines it deep inside it, even though we know that the mirror has no such depth.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. It presents the second major theory of vision: that a radiative body (jism shu'ā'ī) exits the eye in the shape of a cone. This is the view of most mathematicians. The author then details three variations: (1) the cone is solid and uniform; (2) it is composed of many thin radiative lines emanating from the eye's center and spreading to the object, so that only parts hit by the lines are seen, explaining why very small objects are invisible; (3) a single thin ray exits and scans the object rapidly. The passage then provides a proof for the emission theory using the example of seeing one's face in a mirror. The argument is that if the image were imprinted in the mirror, it would be fixed and not change with the viewer's position; instead, the image changes because a ray from the eye reflects off the mirror to the face. The changing apparent depth of the image (near the surface when close, deeper when far) also supports this, since the mirror has no actual depth.

Key Terms
الشعاع (al-shu'ā': ray)
المخروط (al-makhruṭ: cone)
الجسم الشعاعي (al-jism al-shu'ā'ī: radiative body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All emission theories say a solid ray exits the eye.
Correction: The three modes show variation: solid cone, bundle of lines, or scanning ray.

Checkpoint
According to the second variation of the emission theory, why are extremely small parts not seen?
A) The cone is solid and blocks them.
B) Only one line scans slowly.
C) They fall between the ends of the radiative lines.
D) The eye's light is too weak.

Key Takeaway
Mathematicians agreed on the emission theory but differed on whether the ray is a solid cone, a bundle of lines, or a scanning ray.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage explains that extremely small parts are hidden from the eye because they fall between the radiative lines. Have you ever considered that your vision has inherent limitations? How does this physical limitation affect your awareness of Allah’s creation, and what does it teach you about relying solely on your senses to grasp the truth?
?
The mathematicians differed on the exact mechanism of vision, yet all affirmed that perception comes from emitted rays. In your own life, when you assume your understanding of a situation is complete, how can this lesson humble you to recognize the partial nature of your perception and push you to seek deeper knowledge beyond what is immediately visible?

The Mirror Argument for Emission Theory
Provides a proof for the emission theory using mirror vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of mirror vision supports the emission theory over the imprint theory.

Core Idea
Changing mirror images prove rays are emitted, not imprinted.

To support their theory, emission theorists used the example of seeing one's face in a mirror. They argued that if images were imprinted in the mirror, they would not change with the viewer's position or distance. This lesson explains their reasoning from the phenomenon of changing image depth.

Guided Path
1
Read the mirror proof passage
Read the Arabic argument carefully. Note the setup: seeing one's face in a mirror.
2
Understand the rejection of imprint theory
The theorists claim the image is not imprinted in the mirror. If it were, it would be fixed and not change with the viewer's position.
This is the key point against the alternative theory.
3
Follow the emission explanation
They say a ray exits the eye, hits the mirror, and reflects to the face. The changing apparent depth (near when close, deep when far) supports this because the mirror has no actual depth.
The argument uses the fact that the mirror is flat but the image seems to move.

Why the Emission Theory of Vision Fails
Presents the third theory and refutes the second and third emission theories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third emission theory of vision and the two arguments used by Islamic theologians to invalidate all emission theories.

Core Idea
A tiny eye's light cannot fill the cosmos—emission theories collapse on distance alone.

This passage presents a third version of the emission theory of vision and then refutes both the second and third versions. The author argues that whether a ray exits the eye or the air becomes conditioned, the theory faces fatal physical objections. You will see two arguments: one based on wind disturbance, and another based on the impossibility of a small eye's light reaching distant stars.

Guided Path
1
Read the third theory
Understand the third theory: no ray exits the eye, but air becomes conditioned. Notice the author dismisses it as a variant of the second.
2
Follow the wind argument
Read the refutation using wind: if wind disturbs the air, rays would bend and cause seeing non-facing objects. Think about the analogy with sound.
3
Examine the sparrow argument
Consider the argument from distance: a sparrow's light cannot affect the air up to the fixed stars. This shows the physical impossibility.
This is the key logical blow.
4
Conclusion
The author concludes that the ray's mediation in vision is absolutely invalid. Reflect on why the argument is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا مذهب ثالث : هو أنه ليس يخرج من العين شعاع لكن الهواء الذي بينها وبين المرئي يتكيف بكيفية الشعاع الذي فيها ويصير ذلك آلة في الإبصار، ولما كان هذا أيضاً مبنياً على الشعاع كان في حكم المذهب الثاني كما مر . ( ويبطله ) أي المذهب الثاني ( أنه إذا كان ) هناك ( ريح ) عاصفة ( أو اضطراب في الهواء وجب أن تتشوش تلك الشعاعات ) الخارجة من العين ) وتتصل بالأشياء الغير المقابلة للوجه، فوجب أن يرى الإنسان ما لا يقابله لاتصال شعاعه به كما أنه لما كان الصوت عبارة عن الكيفية التي يحملها الهواء المتموج لا جرم أنه يضطرب عند هبوب الرياح ويميل من جهة إلى جهة ) وأشار إلى إبطاله وإبطال المذهب الثالث معاً بقوله : وأيضاً فتعلم ضرورة أن النور الذي يخرج من عين العصفور يستحيل أن يؤثر فيما بينه وبين الكواكب الثابتة ) أي يستحيل أن يقوى ذلك النور على خرق الهواء والأفلاك بحيث يصل إلى الثوابت ويتصل بنصف كرة العالم، ويستحيل أيضاً أن يقوى نور عينه على إحالة ما بينهما إلى كيفيته . بل نقول : ذلك العصفور أو الإنسان أو الفيل إن كان كله نوراً لما امتد ولا أحال ) إلى كيفيته ( من الهواء عشرة فراسخ وإن لم يكن هذا جلياً في العقل فلا جلي عنده) وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يتصور امتداده إلى الثوابت ولا إحالة الشعاع الذي في العين ما بينهما إلى جوهره فبطل القول بالشعاع وتوسطه في الإبصار مطلقاً .
And here is a third doctrine: that no ray exits the eye, but the air between it and the visible object becomes qualified with the quality of the ray that is in it, and that becomes an instrument for vision. Since this too is based on the ray, it falls under the second doctrine as mentioned. (It invalidates) i.e., the second doctrine (that if there is) a stormy wind (or disturbance in the air, those rays) exiting the eye (must be disturbed) and connect to things not facing the face, so a person would see what is not facing him because his ray connects to it, just as since sound is a quality carried by undulating air, it is inevitably disturbed when winds blow and inclines from one direction to another). And he indicated the invalidation of it and of the third doctrine together by his saying: 'Also, you know necessarily that the light that exits from the eye of a sparrow cannot possibly affect what is between it and the fixed stars' i.e., it is impossible for that light to be strong enough to penetrate the air and spheres so as to reach the fixed stars and connect with half the sphere of the world. It is also impossible for the light of its eye to be strong enough to change what is between them to its own quality. Rather, we say: that sparrow, or human, or elephant, even if it were entirely light, would not extend nor change (to its quality) ten leagues of air; if this is not evident in the intellect, then nothing is evident. And if this is the case, its extension to the fixed stars nor the alteration of what is between them to the substance of the ray in the eye can be conceived. Thus the doctrine of the ray and its mediation in vision is invalidated absolutely.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents a third theory and then refutes the second and third emission theories. The third theory holds that no ray exits the eye; rather, the air between the eye and object becomes conditioned by the ray's quality and serves as the instrument. Since this still involves a ray, it is considered a variant of the second theory. The refutation of the second theory uses two arguments: (1) If wind disturbs the air, the rays would be disrupted and connect to non-facing objects, making us see things not in front of us, analogous to sound bending in wind. (2) The light from a small eye like a sparrow cannot possibly affect the vast distance to the fixed stars or even ten leagues. Therefore, the entire emission theory is invalidated. The argument emphasizes the impossibility of a small eye's light transforming a huge medium, and concludes that the claim of a ray mediating vision is false.

Key Terms
الشعاع (al-shu'ā': ray)
التكييف (al-takyīf: conditioning)
الهواء (al-hawā': air)
الكواكب الثابتة (al-kawākib al-thābita: fixed stars)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Emission theories are plausible because a ray from the eye can reach distant objects.
Correction: The light from a small eye cannot traverse vast distances or affect a medium, as shown by the example of a sparrow's eye.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the emission theory is invalid based on the example of seeing a sparrow's eye?
A) The sparrow's eye is too small to emit any light.
B) The sparrow's light cannot reach the fixed stars due to the great distance and cannot transform the medium.
C) The sparrow's eye does not have a ray.
D) The sparrow can only see close objects.

Key Takeaway
Emission theories of vision are invalid because a small eye cannot affect a vast medium, and wind would cause seeing non-facing objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses a simple logical argument to dismantle a once-popular theory. What beliefs do you hold that you have never subjected to such rigorous scrutiny, and will you now? Can you name one and commit to testing it with clear evidence?
?
When you consider that even the most powerful light from a creature cannot affect ten leagues of air, how does this humble your own perception of your power and influence? In what areas of your life do you assume you have an effect far beyond what is possible, and how will you realign that assumption with reality?
?
The third theory tried to escape the problems of the second by modifying it, but it still relied on the same flawed assumption. Where in your spiritual life have you made small adjustments to avoid confronting a deeper error? What if the only solution is to abandon the assumption entirely?

Proof of the Inverted Tree Image in Water
Al-Jurjani proves why the tree appears inverted via reflection geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani geometrically proves that the inverted image of a tree in water is a necessary consequence of the law of reflection, and why alternative configurations are impossible.

Core Idea
The tree's inversion in water is a necessary consequence of reflection geometry, not an optical illusion.

Al-Jurjani presents a subtle geometric proof to show that the inverted image of a tree in water follows necessarily from the equality of the angles of incidence and reflection. He uses a construction of lines and points to demonstrate that the reflected rays cause the top of the tree to appear deeper in the water. This passage illustrates how kalam scholars integrated geometry into their causal explanations.

Guided Path
1
Set up the geometric construction
Read the passage and identify the key elements: the river width AB, the tree HB, the pupil of the eye H, and points K, W on AB and H, T on HB. Understand how the rays are drawn.
The construction is the foundation of the proof.
2
Follow the law of reflection at work
Notice how the radial angles equal the reflective angles at points W and K, leading to longer reflected rays for the top of the tree. Understand why the soul perceives the top as deeper.
The equality of angles is the key causal principle.
3
See why other reflection paths are impossible
Al-Jurjani then rules out alternative paths that would lead to contradictions, using angle comparisons. Grasp the logical necessity of the correct configuration.
This part verifies the uniqueness of the reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما رؤية الشجر منتكساً فمن لوازم تساوي زاويتي الشعاع والانعكاس، ولنشر إليه هاهنا إشارة خفية وهي أن نفرض خط اب عرض النهر وخط حب الشجر القائم على و هـ الحدقة ونفرض على ا ب نقطتي كـ و ، و على حب نقطتي حط فإذا خرج من هـ خط شعاعي إلى و، وآخر إلى كـ وجب أن ينعكس الأول إلى نقطة ط مثلاً فتكون الزاوية الشعاعية أعني زاوية هـ و ا كالزاوية الانعكاسية أعني زاوية ط و ب وأن ينعكس الآخر إلى نقطة حفيتساوى أيضاً شعاعية هـ و ا وانعكاسية ح ك ب حتى تكون الخطوط المنعكسة من سطح الماء إلى الشجر كأوتار الآلة الحدباء المسماة بجنك على ما مر في ذلك المرصد، فيكون المنعكس إلى رأس الشجر أطول من المنعكس إلى ما تحته ولا شعور للنفس بالانعكاس لاعتيادها الرؤية بخروج الأشعة على الاستقامة فيكون رأس الشجر عندها أدخل في عمق الماء، وهكذا إلى أسفله فتراه منتكساً رأسه أبعد من سطح الماء غائر فيه جداً، ولا يجوز أن ينعكس الخط ك إلى ط، ومن و إلى حوإلا كانت شعاعية هـ و ا كانعكاسية ط و ب وهذه الانعكاسية أصغر من زاوية ح و ب الخارجة عن مثلث رك و فشعاعية هـ و ا أصغر أيضاً من هذه الخارجة. ثم نقول زاوية هـ ك ا أكبر للعلة المذكورة من زاوية هـ و ا المساوية لزاوية ح ب و فتكون أكبر منها أيضاً فيلزم أن يكون كل من زاويتي هـ كـاح و ب أكبر من الأخرى هذا خلف. وإما أنه لا يجوز أن ينعكس من نقطة واحد كـك مثلاً خطان إلى نقطتين من الشجر كنقطتي حاط فلاستلزامه مساواة الكل والجزء لشيء واحد كما لا يخفى. قوله: ( وهذه الانعكاسية أصغر من زاوية (ج) د ب الخارجة عن مثلث (زد و )) وذلك لأنه لو لم تكن هذه الانعكاسية أصغر من زاوية ( ج د ب ) لم يوجد المثلث المذكور لأنه حينئذ لم يوجد أضلاع ذلك المثلث إذ لم يكن خط ( و ح) ضلعاً له بل يقع نقطة (ح) بين نقطتي (ط ب فيكون الانعكاس صحيحاً والمقدر خلافه . وقوله : للعلة المذكورة أي لمثل العلة المذكورة يعني أن زاوية ( هـ دا يكون ح أكبر من زاوية (هـ) و ا لأنها خارجة عن مثلث ( هـ و د) فلو لم تكن أكبر منها لم تكن خط (دو) ضلعاً من أضلاع هذا المثلث كما لا يخفى على من له تخيل صحيح.
As for seeing the tree inverted, it is a necessary consequence of the equality of the two angles of the ray and the reflection. We will allude to it here with a subtle indication: we assume the line AB is the width of the river, and the line HB is the tree standing on the bank, and W is the pupil; we assume on AB points K and W, and on HB points H and T. When a radial line goes out from H to W, and another to K, the first must reflect to point T, for example, so the radial angle, i.e., angle H W A, equals the reflective angle, i.e., angle T W B; and the other must reflect to point H, so also the radial angle H W A equals the reflective angle H K B, such that the lines reflected from the water surface to the tree are like the strings of the arched instrument called the jank (harp), as mentioned in that observatory. Thus the reflection to the top of the tree is longer than the reflection to what is below it, and the soul is unaware of the reflection due to its habit of seeing with rays going straight, so the top of the tree is perceived as deeper in the water, and so on downwards, so it sees it inverted, its head farther from the water surface, deeply submerged. It is not permissible for the line K to reflect to T, and from W to H, unless the radial angle H W A equals the reflective angle T W B, and this reflective angle is smaller than angle H W B outside the triangle R K W, so the radial angle H W A is also smaller than this external angle. Then we say: angle H K A is larger, due to the mentioned cause, than angle H W A which is equal to angle H B W, so it is also larger than it, leading to each of the two angles H K A and H B being larger than the other—a contradiction. Moreover, it is not permissible for two lines to reflect from a single point, such as K, to two points on the tree, such as H and T, because it necessitates equality of the whole and the part for one thing, as is obvious. His statement: (And this reflective angle is smaller than angle J D B outside the triangle Z D W) — that is because if this reflective angle were not smaller than angle J D B, the mentioned triangle would not exist, because then the sides of that triangle would not exist, since line W H would not be a side of it, but point H would lie between points T and B, so the reflection would be correct, contrary to what is assumed. And his statement: 'due to the mentioned cause' means similar to the mentioned cause, i.e., angle H D A K is larger than angle H W A because it is external to triangle H W D; if it were not larger, line D W would not be a side of this triangle, as is clear to one with sound imagination.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a geometric proof explaining why a tree standing on a riverbank appears inverted when reflected in water. The author (al-Jurjani) presents a subtle indication (إشارة خفية) to demonstrate that the inversion is a necessary consequence of the law of reflection, not a visual illusion. He begins by setting up a geometric construction: line AB represents the width of the river, line HB the tree, and point H (?) the pupil of the eye. He then defines points K and W on AB, and points H and T on HB. Two radial lines emerge from H: one to W and one to K. By the law of reflection, the first reflects to T such that angle HWA (angle of incidence) equals angle TWB (angle of reflection). Similarly, the second reflects to H such that angle HWA equals angle HKB (though careful: the text says 'H K B' but likely means the reflective angle at K). The result is that the reflected rays from the water surface to the tree behave like the strings of a curved instrument (the jank/harp). Because the reflection to the top of the tree is longer than to the lower parts, and the soul is unaware of the reflection (since it is accustomed to direct vision), it perceives the top as deeper in the water, giving an inverted image. The proof then considers a potential contradiction: if the ray from K to T and from W to H were allowed, it would require the reflective angle at W to be smaller than an external angle, leading to an impossible situation (two angles each being larger than the other). Finally, he notes that two rays cannot reflect from a single point (e.g., K) to two different points on the tree (H and T) because that would imply equality of the whole and the part. The commentator then clarifies the geometric reasoning: the external angle condition ensures the triangle's existence, and the 'mentioned cause' refers to the external angle theorem. This passage showcases the integration of geometry into kalam cosmology.

Key Terms
رؤية الشجر منتكساً
تساوي زاويتي الشعاع والانعكاس
الانعكاس
الشعاع
الحدقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inversion is due to refraction (انعطاف) of light in water.
Correction: The inversion is not due to refraction but to reflection (انعكاس) of light from the water surface, as proven by the equality of angles.

Checkpoint
Why does the tree appear inverted in water according to the author?
A) Because of refraction of light in water
B) Because the reflected rays from the top of the tree are longer than from the lower parts, and the soul ignores the reflection
C) Because the water surface is curved
D) Because the observer's eye is not positioned correctly

Key Takeaway
The inverted tree image in water is a necessary consequence of the law of reflection and geometric necessity, not a visual illusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Jurjani says the soul is unaware of the reflection because it is accustomed to seeing via straight rays. In what areas of your faith or worldview are you relying on habit rather than deep understanding? What is one assumption you hold that might be overturned if you examined it more closely?

Definition and Mechanism of Hearing
Al-Jurjani defines hearing and describes its physical mechanism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defines the sense of hearing and explains its physical mechanism in terms of compressed air, a nerve, and a power.

Core Idea
Hearing is a power in a nerve that perceives sound carried by compressed air.

Al-Jurjani now turns to the second sensor: hearing. He provides a concise definition and then a detailed mechanism rooted in the physical interaction of air and a specialized nerve. This passage demonstrates how kalam scholars integrated natural philosophy into their analysis of sensory perception, grounding the act of hearing in a specific bodily organ with a particular structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the flow and key terms.
Notice that the passage begins with a definition, then describes the causal chain.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the definition of hearing as 'the auditory power.' Then note the elements: compressed air, ear canal, nerve with confined air like a drum.
The nerve is described as having air confined inside it, similar to a drum.
3
Trace the causal chain
Follow the sequence: striker and struck produce compressed air → air reaches ear canal → air strikes the nerve → power in nerve perceives sound.
4
Understand the failure condition
The passage concludes that if the nerve deviates or loses sensitivity, hearing ceases. This highlights the nerve's crucial role.
This shows that hearing is not merely about sound waves entering the ear, but about a specific physiological structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشعر الثاني : السمع أي القوة السامعة ( وإنما يحصل) الإدراك السمعي كما سلف بوصول الهواء المنضغط بين القارع والمقروع إلى الصماخ لقوة حاصلة في العصبة المفروشة في مؤخره التي فيها هواء محتقن كالطبل ) فإذا وصل الهواء الحامل للصوت إلى تلك العصبة وقرعها أدركته القوة المودعة فيها، فإذا انحرفت تلك العصبة أو بطل حسها بطل السمع .
The second sensor: hearing, which is the auditory power. Auditory perception occurs, as previously, by the arrival of the air compressed between the striker and the struck to the ear canal (صماخ), due to a power present in the nerve spread in its back, which contains air confined like a drum. When the air carrying the sound reaches that nerve and strikes it, the power deposited in it perceives it. If that nerve deviates or its sensitivity ceases, hearing ceases.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of hearing (السمع) as the auditory power (القوة السامعة). Al-Jurjani then explains the mechanism: auditory perception happens when compressed air (الهواء المنضغط) from the collision between the striker (القارع) and the struck (المقروع) reaches the ear canal (الصماخ). This is due to a power inherent in a nerve (العصبة) spread at the back of the ear, which contains air confined like a drum (كالطبل). When the air carrying the sound reaches and strikes that nerve, the power deposited in the nerve perceives it. The passage concludes that if this nerve becomes displaced (انحرفت) or its sensitivity (حسها) is lost, hearing ceases. The emphasis is on the physical basis of hearing through air compression and a specialized nerve, reflecting the kalam integration of natural philosophy with sensory psychology.

Correction: According to al-Jurjani, hearing requires a specific nerve with confined air and a power deposited in it; without the nerve's proper function, hearing ceases.

Checkpoint

C) Absence of air between the striker and struck

Smell: Definition and Two Theories
Al-Jurjani defines smell and presents two conflicting theories on how odors are perceived.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic philosophers defined the sense of smell and the two competing theories about how odors are perceived.

Core Idea
Smell is a power in the forebrain, but its mechanism is debated: particles vs. qualities.

Al-Jurjani introduces the sense of smell and its physical seat in the brain. He then presents two conflicting views from philosophers on how odors actually reach the olfactory power. This passage sets up a foundational debate in medieval theories of perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get a clear sense of the definition and the two theories.
Notice the parentheses: they mark the original matn (al-Mawaqif) quoted by al-Jurjani.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms 'تحلل' (dissolution), 'تكيف' (qualification), and 'الكيفية' (quality). These are central to the two theories.
The first theory uses 'تحلل' and 'تبخر'; the second uses 'يتكيف'.
3
Compare the two theories
The first theory claims odors travel by particles mixing with air. The second claims air becomes qualified with the odor without particle transfer. Note the difference in mechanism.
This contrast is important for understanding causality in perception.
4
Reflect on the debate's significance
Ask yourself: why does it matter whether particles travel or qualities propagate? This relates to whether physical contact is necessary for sensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشعر الثالث : الشم) وهو قوة مستودعة في زائدتين في مقدم الدماغ كحلمتي الثدي وزعم بعضهم أن الرائحة تتأدى إليه ) أي إلى هذا المشعر ) بتحلل أجزاء من الجسم ذي الرائحة وتبخره ومخالطته للمتوسط ) من الهواء بين القوة الشامة وذلك الجسم ( وزعم آخرون أن الهواء المتوسط ( يتكيف بتلك الكيفية ) الأقرب فالأقرب إلى أن يصل إلى ما يجاور محل هذه القوة فيدركها ( من غير أن يخالطه شيء من أجزاء ذي الرائحة
The third sensor: smell. It is a power deposited in two protrusions in the forebrain, like the nipples of the breast. Some claimed that the odor is transmitted to it (i.e., to this sensor) by the dissolution of parts of the scented body, its evaporation, and its mixing with the medium of air between the olfactory power and that body. Others claimed that the medium air becomes qualified with that quality, (approaching) step by step until it reaches what is adjacent to the place of this power, and it perceives it, without any parts of the scented body mixing with it.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of smell (الشم) as a power (قوة) deposited in two protrusions (زائدتين) in the forebrain, resembling the nipples of the breast (كحلمتي الثدي). Al-Jurjani then presents two competing theories regarding how odors are perceived: 1) Some (بعضهم) claim that the odor is transmitted by the dissolution (تحلل) of parts of the scented body, its evaporation (تبخر), and its mixing (مخالطة) with the intervening air between the olfactory power and the object. 2) Others (آخرون) claim that the intervening air becomes qualified (يتكيف) with that quality (الكيفية), and this quality propagates step by step (الأقرب فالأقرب) until it reaches the vicinity of the olfactory power, which then perceives it, without any actual parts of the scented body mixing with the air. The passage sets up the two positions that will be critically examined in the following sections.

Correction: The second theory holds that smell can occur through qualitative transmission of the medium without any particle mixing.

Key Takeaway
The sense of smell is a power in the forebrain, and two classical theories explain odor perception: one via particle transfer, the other via quality transmission without particles.

Proof for the Second Theory of Smell
Al-Jurjani provides arguments for the second theory and concludes with the musk example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments for the theory that smell is transmitted by qualitative qualification of the air, and why the musk example supports this while leaving room for both mechanisms.

Core Idea
Odor travels by qualitative impact; musk's lasting weight proves no particles are lost.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents evidence for the second theory of smell, which holds that odor is transmitted by affecting the air qualitatively rather than by dispersing particles. He offers two compelling arguments: one based on the gradation of odor with distance, and another based on the observation that musk perfumes many places without losing weight. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and concludes with a nuanced point about the compatibility of both theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the two main arguments separated by the musk example.
The parentheses in the Arabic indicate the musk argument.
2
Analyze the distance argument
The first argument: the farther the scented object, the weaker the odor. This is because each air particle is affected by its neighbor, and the effect is always weaker. Consider why this supports qualitative transmission over particle dispersal.
Gradation implies a chain of causation, not discrete particles.
3
Examine the musk argument
The second argument: a small piece of musk perfumes many places for a long time without losing weight. Ask yourself: why does this contradict the idea that odor comes from particles dissolving?
If particles were leaving, the weight would decrease.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that this proof invalidates the exclusive claim of the first theory, but does not rule out that smell may occur by either mechanism sometimes. Notice the balanced approach.
The author is not rejecting the first theory entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيد ذلك بأن ذا الرائحة كلما كان أبعد كانت الرائحة المدركة أضعف لأن كل جزء من الهواء إنما ينفعل بالرائحة من مجاوره ولا شك أن كيفية المتأثر أضعف من كيفية المؤثر ( وهذا هو الحق لأن المسك ) القليل ( يعطر مواضع كثيرة ويدوم ذلك مدة بقائه ولا يقل وزنه ) مما كان ولو كان ذلك يتحلل منه لامتنع ذلك. وأنت تعلم أن هذا إنما يبطل انحصار الشم في الوجه الأول ولا ينافي حصوله على كل واحد من الوجهين تارة معاً وتارة بدلاً عن الآخر كما ذكره بعض المحققين
And he supported that (i.e., the second theory) by the fact that the scented object, the farther it is, the weaker the perceived odor, because each part of the air is only affected by the odor from its neighbor; and undoubtedly the quality of the affected thing is weaker than the quality of the affecting thing. And this is the truth, because a little musk perfumes many places and that continues for the duration of its existence, and its weight does not decrease from what it was. If that (odor) were due to dissolution from it, that would be impossible. And you know that this only invalidates the restriction of smell to the first theory, but does not preclude its occurrence by either of the two ways, sometimes together and sometimes alternately, as mentioned by some investigators.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for the second theory of smell (that odor is transmitted by qualitative qualification of the air without particle transfer). The author gives two arguments: 1) The farther the scented object, the weaker the perceived odor. This is because each part of the air is affected (ينفعل) by the odor from its neighbor, and the quality of the affected is always weaker than the quality of the cause. This gradation is more consistent with successive qualification than with particle dispersal. 2) The musk example: a small piece of musk perfumes many places for a long time without losing weight. If odor were due to the dissolution (تحلل) of particles, the musk would lose weight, which is not observed. The author then concludes that this is the truth (وهذا هو الحق). However, he adds a nuanced point: this proof only invalidates the exclusive claim of the first theory (انحصار الشم في الوجه الأول), but it does not preclude that smell could occur by either mechanism, sometimes together, sometimes alternately, as mentioned by some investigators. This shows a careful approach, not rejecting the first theory entirely but arguing against its exclusivity.

كيفية المتأثر
المسك

انحصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The musk argument proves that the second theory is the only correct theory of smell.
Correction: The author explicitly states that this argument only invalidates the restriction of smell to the first theory, but does not preclude smell from occurring by either mechanism.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
