Two Objections to the Dissolution Theory of Smell and Their Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents two objections by proponents of the dissolution theory and refutes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key objections raised against the dissolution theory of smell and how Al-Jurjani refutes them by offering alternative explanations based on preparation of the air or organ, and by distinguishing between mere odor spreading and physical dissolution.

Core Idea
Heat and rubbing prepare the air or organ for smell; the apple withers from breath and touch, not from odor loss.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani presents two arguments from those who believe smell occurs by physical dissolution of particles. Each argument is met with a careful refutation that shifts the causal explanation away from dissolution toward qualitative preparation. By examining these dialectical exchanges, we see how kalam thinkers test and refine theories about natural processes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Note the comment at the end about rhetorical parallelism.
2
Identify the first objection and reply
Find the first argument (heat/rubbing) and note how the author denies the conclusion and offers an alternative explanation involving preparation (تعد) of the air or organ.
The author uses 'لا نسلم' to reject the causal link.
3
Identify the second objection and reply
Find the apple withering argument and see how the author attributes it to breath and touch, not to mere odor spreading. Understand the logical consequence: if odor spreading caused dissolution, there would be no difference between smelling and not smelling.
This shows a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyse the final gloss
Look at the commentator's explanation of 'يذكيها' and the chiasmus (لف ونشر) between 'إدراكاً واتصافاً' and 'الشامة والأهوية المتوسطة'.
This clarifies the rhetorical structure of the reply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الأولون بوجهين. الأول : إن الحرارة تهيج (الروائح) وتثيرها وكذلك كل من الدلك والتبخير يذكيها وينشرها والبرد يكثفها ويخفيها فدل ذلك على أن الشم بالتحلل (قلنا :) لا نسلم ما ذكرتم (بل) الحرارة وأخواتها ( تعدها ) أي تعد الشامة والأهوية المتوسطة بينها وبين ذي الرائحة لقبول الرائحة إدراكاً واتصافاً وذلك إما (لتأثيرها في الهواء) وإعدادها إياه للاتصاف بالرائحة ( أو ) تأثيرها ( في الآلة ) وإعدادها للشم. (الثاني التفاحة تذبل من كثرة الشم) فلولا أنه يتحلل شيء منها لم يكن كذلك. قلنا :) ( ليس ذبولها من كثرته بل من وصول النفس إليها . وكثرة اللمس ) فإنهما يحللانها ( وأما مجرد انتشار (الرائحة ) منها ( فلا ) يحللها ( وإلا لم يتفاوت) مع الانتشار ( الشم وعدمه ) وهو باطل قطعاً . قوله : ( يدكها) الذك الدق والكسر ، وقوله : إدراكاً واتصافاً نشر على ترتيب اللف المذكور أعني قوله : بعد الشامة والأهوية المتوسطة.
The first group argued with two points. First: Heat excites and arouses odors, and likewise rubbing and vaporization make them sharp and spread them, while cold condenses and conceals them. That indicates that smell occurs by dissolution. We said: We do not accept what you mentioned. Rather, heat and its sisters prepare (تعد) the olfactory organ and the intermediate air between it and the scented object for receiving the odor in perception and description. That is either by their effect on the air, preparing it for being described with the odor, or by their effect on the instrument, preparing it for smelling. Second: The apple withers from excessive smelling; if not for the dissolution of something from it, it would not be so. We said: Its withering is not from excessive smelling, but from the arrival of breath to it and from frequent touching, for these two dissolve it. As for mere spreading of the odor from it, it does not dissolve it; otherwise, there would be no difference with spreading between smelling and not smelling - which is absolutely false. His statement: (يذكيها) means 'الذك' is pounding and breaking. His statement: 'perception and description' is an arrangement [chiasmus] of the previous parallelism, i.e., his statement: 'after the olfactory organ and the intermediate air.'
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (وجهين) raised by proponents of the first theory (that smell occurs by dissolution of particles) and the author's replies. First objection: Heat excites and spreads odors, while cold condenses them, which suggests that smell depends on the movement of particles (dissolution). Reply: The author denies this conclusion (لا نسلم). Instead, heat and similar factors prepare (تعد) either the air or the olfactory instrument (الآلة) for receiving and perceiving the odor, not necessarily by causing actual dissolution. Second objection: An apple withers when smelled frequently, implying that some particles are lost. Reply: The withering is not due to the spreading of odor (انتشار الرائحة) alone, but because of the breath (النفس) reaching the apple and frequent touching (كثرة اللمس), which physically break it down. Mere odor spreading does not cause dissolution; if it did, there would be no difference between sniffing and not sniffing - which is obviously false. The passage ends with a commentator's gloss explaining the word 'يذكيها' and noting the rhetorical parallelism ('إدراكاً واتصافاً' is a reversed order of the earlier 'الشامة والأهوية المتوسطة'). This lesson shows the dialectical method of kalam in testing philosophical claims.

Key Terms
تحلل
يهيج
يذكي
الشامة
الآلة
التفاحة
يدكها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heat and rubbing necessarily cause dissolution of the scented substance.
Correction: Heat and rubbing prepare the air or the olfactory organ for receiving the odor, not necessarily causing dissolution.

Checkpoint
What is the author's reply to the objection that the apple withers from frequent smelling?
A) The apple withers because of the dissolution of its particles due to smell
B) The apple withers due to the arrival of breath and frequent touching, not from mere odor spreading
C) The apple withers because it is a special quality of apples
D) The apple does not actually wither

Key Takeaway
The dissolution theory is challenged by showing that apparent signs of dissolution (heat's effect, apple withering) can be explained by alternative mechanisms without actual particle loss.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage shows that the apple's withering is not due to the loss of its odor but to external factors (breath and touch). In your own life, what 'withering' have you attributed to the wrong cause? How might you be misinterpreting the trials you face as punishments or losses, when they might be caused by something else entirely? Will you ask God to show you the real cause before you despair?
?
The author argues that heat and rubbing 'prepare' the air or organ for perception, not cause dissolution. How does this distinction affect your understanding of how God's creation functions? Are you willing to admit that some things you attribute to physical necessity may actually be prepared by divine wisdom? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that every natural process is an act of preparation by Allah?

Vapor and Smoke: The Building Blocks of Atmospheric Phenomena
Sharh al-Mawaqif explains vapor and smoke and their role in generating atmospheric phenomena.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of vapor and smoke according to classical Islamic physics, and how their mixture and interaction with temperature layers produce rain, snow, hail, and fog.

Core Idea
Vapor and smoke are the building blocks of all atmospheric phenomena.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a detailed account of how the sun's heat raises aerial and watery parts as vapor, and fiery and earthy parts as smoke. These rarely rise pure; their mixture gives rise to clouds, rain, snow, hail, and fog. The author also clarifies a common misconception about the nature of smoke.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the technical terms for vapor (بخار) and smoke (دخان).
2
Identify definitions of vapor and smoke
The passage defines vapor as a mixture of aerial and watery parts (rising heavy) and smoke as fiery and earthly parts (rising light). Note that smoke is not limited to black smoke from fire.
3
Trace the conditions for atmospheric phenomena
Follow the two scenarios for vapor: little vapor with intense heat (dissolves) vs. abundant vapor (may condense into clouds). Then note the role of the freezing layer (الطبقة الزمهريرية) in producing rain, snow, or hail depending on cold intensity.
Pay attention to the distinction between snow (freezing before collection) and hail (freezing after collection).
4
Understand fog and the Ibn Sina observation
When abundant vapor does not condense into a cloud, it forms fog. Ibn Sina's observation illustrates a cloud forming from vapor rising slightly from a mountain and raining on people below.
The passage uses 'مكبة' to describe the cloud shape.
5
Reflect on textual notes
The end contains a textual correction from 'chapters of the first purpose' to 'chapters of the first observatory' and explanations of unusual terms. This shows careful transmission.
This is a typical feature of commentaries (sharh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس : من المركبات البخار والدخان من الأقسام الخمسة التي ينطوي عليها الفصل الثاني من فصول المرصد الأول من موقف الجواهر فلا يستبعد ورود الخامس عقيب الثالث في المركبات التي مزاج لها . اعلم أن حر الشمس وغيرها يصعد إلى الجو أجزاء إما هوائية ومائية مختلطتين وهو البخار وصعوده ثقيل وإما نارية وأرضية وهو الدخان وصعوده خفيف، وليس ينحصر الدخان كما تعورف في الجسم الأسود الذي يرتفع مما يحترق بالنار، وقلما يصعد البخار والدخان ساذجا بل يتصاعدان في الأغلب ممتزجين ومنهما يتكون الآثار العلوية، أما البخار فإن قل واشتد الحر في الهواء حلل الأجزاء المائية وقلبها إلى الهوائية وبقي الهواء الصرف وإلا أي وإن لم يكن الأمر كذلك بل كان البخار كثيراً ولم يكن في الهواء من الحرارة ما يحلله فإن وصل ذلك البخار بصعوده إلى الطبقة الزمهريرية التي هي الهواء البارد كما عرفت عقده ببرده وتكاثف فصار سحاباً وتقاطرت الأجزاء المائية إما بلا جمود إذا لم يكن البرد شديداً وهو المطر وإما مع جمود إذا كان البرد شديداً فإن كان الجمود قبل الاجتماع والتقاطر وصيرورته حبات كباراً فهو الثلج وإن كان الجمود بعده فهو البرد وإنما يستدير ويصير كالكرة بالحركة السريعة الخارقة للهواء بمصادمته فتنمحي الزوايا عن جوانب القطرات المنجمدة وإن لم يصل البخار بالتصاعد إلى الزمهريرية فإما أن يكون كثيراً أو قليلاً، فالكثيرة قد تنعقد سحاباً ماطراً كما حكى ابن سينا أنه شاهد البخار قد صعد من أسافل بعض الجبال صعوداً يسيراً وتكاثف حتى كأنه مكبة موضوعة على وهدة فكان هو فوق تلك الغمامة في الشمس، وكان من تحتها من أهل القرية التي كانت هناك يمطرون وقد لا ينعقد فهو أي هذا البخار الكثير المتكاثف الذي لم ينعقد سحاباً ماطراً الضباب المجاور لوجه الأرض وأما قليله أي قليل البخار الذي لم يصل إلى تلك الطبقة قوله : ( من فصول المقصد الأول هكذا وجدنا في النسخ والصواب أي يقال : من فصول المرصد الأولى أي من فصل المرصد الأول فتأمل . قوله : (كأنه مكبة) أي كان ذلك البخار عمامة مكبة أو داية مكية وهو متراكم على وجهه وقوله وعلى وهدة الوهدة المكان المطمئن. قوله : وكان هو أي ابن سينا. قوله : يمطرون على صيغة المبني للمفعول .
The fifth section: of the compounds: vapor and smoke. It is among the five sections contained in the second chapter of the chapters of the first observatory from the station of substances; so do not find it strange that the fifth follows the third concerning compounds that have a mixture. Know that the heat of the sun and other things raises to the atmosphere parts that are either aerial and watery mixed together — that is vapor, and its rising is heavy — or fiery and earthly — that is smoke, and its rising is light. Smoke is not limited, as commonly understood, to the black body that rises from what burns with fire; vapor and smoke rarely rise pure, but mostly rise mixed. From them are formed the celestial phenomena. As for vapor, if it is little and the heat in the air is intense, it dissolves the watery parts and changes them into aerial ones, and pure air remains. Otherwise (i.e., if it is not so but rather the vapor is abundant and there is not in the air enough heat to dissolve it), then if that vapor reaches by its rising the freezing layer (al-tabaqa al-zamhaririyya), which is cold air as you know, it congeals it by its cold and condenses, becoming a cloud, and the watery parts drip either without freezing (if the cold is not intense) — that is rain — or with freezing (if the cold is intense). If the freezing is before the collection and dripping and its becoming large grains, it is snow. If the freezing is after it, it is hail. And it becomes round and like a ball due to its rapid motion cutting through the air by its collision, so the angles are erased from the sides of the frozen droplets. If the vapor does not reach the freezing layer by rising, then it is either abundant or little. The abundant may become a rain cloud, as Ibn Sina reported that he saw vapor rising from the lower parts of some mountains a slight ascent and condense until it was like a large pot (mikabba) placed on a depression; he was above that cloud in the sun, and those below it from the people of the village that was there were being rained upon. And it may not condense: that is fog adjacent to the earth's surface. As for its little (i.e., the little vapor that does not reach that layer)... His saying: 'from the chapters of the first purpose (al-maqsad) — thus we found it in the copies; correct is: from the chapters of the first observatory (al-marsad), i.e., from the chapter of the first observatory, so reflect.' His saying: 'as if it were a mikabba' means that vapor was a folded turban or a Mikiyya covering, piled up on its face. His saying: 'on a depression' (wahda) is the low place. His saying: 'he was' means Ibn Sina. His saying: 'they were being rained upon' in the passive voice.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth section on compound substances: vapor and smoke. It begins by situating this section within the book's structure: it is one of five sections in the second chapter of the first observatory from the station of substances, so the reader should not be surprised that the fifth follows the third. The topic is compounds that have a mixture. Then it defines vapor and smoke: the heat of the sun raises parts to the atmosphere; if they are aerial and watery mixed, it is vapor (rising heavy); if fiery and earthy, it is smoke (rising light). Smoke is not limited to black smoke from fire. They usually rise mixed. From them, celestial phenomena (al-athar al-‘ulwiyya) are formed. The passage then details the behavior of vapor: if little and intense heat, it dissolves watery parts into air; otherwise, if abundant and insufficient heat, if it reaches the freezing layer (al-zamhaririyya) it condenses into clouds and produces rain, snow, or hail depending on cold intensity and timing of freezing. The rounding of hail is explained by rapid motion. If vapor does not reach the freezing layer, it may form a rain cloud (as Ibn Sina observed) or form fog. The passage ends with a commentator's notes on textual variants and explanations of specific terms like 'mikabba' (a large pot or folded turban) and 'wahda' (depression). The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'from the chapters of the first purpose' should be corrected to 'from the chapters of the first observatory'.

Key Terms

الدخان
الطبقة الزمهريرية
السحاب
المطر
الثلج
البرد
الضباب
الآثار العلوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Smoke is only the black soot from fire.
Correction: The passage states that smoke is not limited to that; it includes any fiery and earthly parts raised by heat, even if not black.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines whether vapor becomes rain, snow, or hail?
A) The altitude of the cloud.
B) The intensity of cold and whether freezing occurs before or after the droplets collect.
C) The amount of smoke mixed with vapor.
D) The direction of the wind.

Key Takeaway
Vapor and smoke, when mixed and subjected to different thermal conditions, are the direct causes of all atmospheric phenomena including rain, snow, hail, and fog.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage explains that smoke is not just black soot but includes any fiery-earthy exhalation. In what areas of your life have you limited your understanding of Allah's creation to only what is obvious? Will you commit to studying His signs more deeply, even when they seem familiar?
?
This lesson shows how a simple cloud results from a delicate balance of heat, cold, and mixture. Where in your own life have you assumed that things 'just happen' without a divine, ordered cause? How can you train your heart to see the Hand of Allah in every natural event, no matter how ordinary?

How Wind is Generated: Causes and Whirlwinds
Describes two causes of wind and formation of whirlwinds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two physical mechanisms of wind generation described in classical Islamic cosmology and how they lead to whirlwinds and cyclones.

Core Idea
Wind is produced either by rising vapors or by the expansion of rarefied air.

The author begins his discussion of wind by identifying its physical causes. He first notes that most winds originate from upward-moving vapors, as experience shows. Then he describes a secondary mechanism: when air becomes rarefied, it expands and pushes against neighboring air, creating a wave-like motion. When winds from different directions collide, they can compress earthly particles, forming rotating whirlwinds and cyclones. Let's read the passage to see how these ideas are presented.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on wind generation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct causes mentioned.
2
Identify the first cause
The first cause is the rising of smoke-like vapors. Why does the author say most principles of winds are upward?
The author refers to experience as evidence.
3
Identify the second cause
The second cause involves rarefaction of air and a chain of pushing. Try to visualize the wave-like propagation.
4
Understand whirlwinds and cyclones
When winds from different directions meet, they compress earthly particles, causing them to twist. This forms whirlwinds and cyclones.
The Persian term 'bikard bad' is mentioned for cyclone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وموضعها فلا يجوز ولذلك الذي ذكرناه من حال الدخان في توليد الريح كان أكثر مبادئ الرياح فوقانية كما تشهد به التجربة والريح كما يحدث بهذا الطريق في الأغلب فقد يحدث أيضاً بأن يتخلخل الهواء فيندفع مكانه بواسطة عظم مقداره فيدافع ما يجاوره فيطاوعه ويدافع ذلك المجاور أيضاً مجاوره فيتموج الهواء وتضعف تلك المدافعة شيئاً فشيئاً إلى غاية ما فيقف وقد يحدث رياح مختلفة الجهة دفعة فتدافع تلك الرياح الأجزاء الأرضية فتنضغط الأجزاء الأرضية بينها مرتفعة كأنها تلتوي على نفسها وهي الزوابع جمع زوبعة وهي الريح المستديرة على نفسها والإعصار المسمى في الفارسية بکرد باد هذا وقد قيل بين الريح والمطر تمانع وتعاون أما التمانع فلأن الريح في الأكثر تلطف مادة السحاب بحرارتها وتفرقها بتحريكها والمطر يبل الأدخنة ويصل بعضها ببعض فيثقل حينئذ ولا يتمكن من الصعود فكل سنة يكثر فيها المطر تقل فيها الريح وبالعكس وأما التعاون فلان المطر يبل الأرض فيعيدها لأن يصعد منها دخان إذ الرطوبة تعين على تحلل اليابس وتصعده والريح تجمع السحاب وتهرب برودة السحاب إلى باطنه فيشتد البرد المكثف وأما مهاب الرياح فغير منحصرة حقيقة في عدد إلا أنهم جعلوا أصولها أربعة هي نقط المشرق والمغرب والشمال والجنوب والعرب تسمي الرياح التي تهب منها بالقبول والدبور والشمال والجنوب وتسمي التي تهب مما بينها نكباء.
قوله جمع زوبعة هي بفتح الزاي المعجمة والباء الموحدة والعين المهملة على وزن الدحرجة مثلا والإعصار ريح يثير الغبار ويرتفع إلى السماء كأنه عمود وقوله نكباء على وزن صحراء والنكباء أيضاً أربع من الرياح بحيث يهب كل واحد منها بين موضعي جنوب اثنين من تلك الأربعة المذكورة أولاً ولكل واحد من هذه الأربعة أيضاً اسم مخصوص على حده كما ذكر في الصحاح
And its place, so it is not permissible to put it in the place of the genus. Therefore, what we mentioned about the state of smoke in generating wind: most principles of winds are upward, as experience testifies. And wind, as it occurs by this path for the most part, may also occur by the air becoming rarefied, then it pushes away its place by means of the greatness of its quantity, then it pushes what is adjacent to it and it complies, and that adjacent also pushes its adjacent, so the air undulates, and that pushing weakens little by little until an extreme, then it stops. And winds may occur from different directions at once, so those winds push the earthly parts, then the earthly parts become compressed between them, rising as if they twist upon themselves—these are the whirlwinds (زوبعة), plural of زوبعة, which is the wind that rotates upon itself, and the cyclone (إعصار), called in Persian 'bikard bad'. And it has been said: between wind and rain there is opposition and cooperation. As for opposition: because wind most often softens the substance of clouds with its heat and disperses them by its movement, while rain moistens the vapors and joins some of them to others, then they become heavy and cannot rise. So whenever a year has much rain, wind is little, and vice versa. As for cooperation: because rain moistens the earth and restores it so that smoke can rise from it, since moisture helps dissolve dry things and make them ascend, and wind gathers clouds and the coldness of clouds escapes to their interior, so the concentrating cold intensifies. As for the directions of winds, they are not actually limited to a number, but they made their origins four: the points of east, west, north, and south. The Arabs call the winds that blow from them: al-qabul (east wind), al-dabur (west wind), al-shamal (north wind), and al-janub (south wind), and they call the winds that blow between them 'nakba'. He said: plural of زوبعة is with fatha on zay, bā, and 'ayn, on the pattern of tadahruja for example. And al-i'ṣār is a wind that raises dust and rises to the sky like a pillar. And he said: nakbā' on the pattern of ṣaḥrā', and nakbā' are also four winds, each one blowing between two southern positions of those four mentioned first, and each of these four also has a specific name as mentioned in al-Ṣiḥāḥ.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage explains the physical generation of wind and its classification. It begins by stating that most winds originate from upward-moving vapors, as experience shows. Then it describes an alternative cause: the rarefaction of air causes it to expand and push against neighboring air, creating a wave-like motion that gradually weakens. When winds from different directions collide, they compress earthly particles, forming whirlwinds (زوبعة) and cyclones (إعصار). The author then discusses the relationship between wind and rain: they oppose each other because wind disperses clouds while rain makes vapors heavy, yet they cooperate because rain moistens the earth to release vapors and wind gathers clouds. Finally, the four cardinal directions (east, west, north, south) are given as the primary origins of winds, with Arab names for each and intermediate winds called 'nakbā'. A marginal note clarifies the linguistic forms of 'زوبعة' and 'نكباء'.

زوبعة

دخان

Misconception & Correction

Correction: This passage shows wind has natural physical causes like rarefaction and collision.

Wind and Rain: Opposition and Cooperation
Explains the opposing and cooperating effects of wind and rain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual relationship between wind and rain—how they oppose each other in some ways and cooperate in others—as explained in classical Islamic cosmology.

Core Idea
Wind and rain both oppose and cooperate: wind disperses clouds, rain moistens vapors; yet rain helps vapor rise and wind gathers clouds.

After discussing the generation of wind, the author examines its relationship with rain. He presents a nuanced view: wind and rain have both opposition (تمانع) and cooperation (تعاون). This shows a balance in natural processes. Let's read the passage to see the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on wind and rain
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two aspects: opposition and cooperation.
2
Understand opposition
Wind disperses clouds with its heat and movement, while rain makes vapors heavy and prevents rising. This explains why rainy years have less wind.
3
Understand cooperation
Rain moistens the earth allowing more vapor to rise, and wind gathers clouds and helps coldness concentrate inside clouds, causing more rain.

Source Text

As for the directions of winds, they are not actually limited to a number, but they made their origins four: the points of east, west, north, and south. The Arabs call the winds that blow from them: al-qabul (east wind), al-dabur (west wind), al-shamal (north wind), and al-janub (south wind), and they call the winds that blow between them 'nakba'. He said: plural of زوبعة is with fatha on zay, bā, and 'ayn, on the pattern of tadahruja for example. And al-i'ṣār is a wind that raises dust and rises to the sky like a pillar. And he said: nakbā' on the pattern of ṣaḥrā', and nakbā' are also four winds, each one blowing between two southern positions of those four mentioned first, and each of these four also has a specific name as mentioned in al-Ṣiḥāḥ.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage explains the physical generation of wind and its classification. It begins by stating that most winds originate from upward-moving vapors, as experience shows. Then it describes an alternative cause: the rarefaction of air causes it to expand and push against neighboring air, creating a wave-like motion that gradually weakens. When winds from different directions collide, they compress earthly particles, forming whirlwinds (زوبعة) and cyclones (إعصار). The author then discusses the relationship between wind and rain: they oppose each other because wind disperses clouds while rain makes vapors heavy, yet they cooperate because rain moistens the earth to release vapors and wind gathers clouds. Finally, the four cardinal directions (east, west, north, south) are given as the primary origins of winds, with Arab names for each and intermediate winds called 'nakbā'. A marginal note clarifies the linguistic forms of 'زوبعة' and 'نكباء'.

Key Terms

تعاون
مطر

سحاب

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one way wind and rain cooperate?
A) Wind disperses clouds and rain makes them heavy
B) Rain moistens the earth so vapors can rise, and wind gathers clouds
C) Wind cools the earth and rain warms it
D) They both come from the same direction

Key Takeaway
Wind and rain have a dual relationship: they oppose each other through dispersal versus moistening, but cooperate through earth moistening and cloud gathering.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that wind and rain—seeming opposites—actually cooperate for a greater purpose. Where in your own life do you experience 'opposing' forces (people, circumstances, inner struggles) that might actually be cooperating for your good if you look with deeper faith? Will you spend five minutes today identifying one such situation and asking Allah to show you the hidden cooperation?
?
The passage says rain moistens the earth so that vapor can rise—a seeming 'weight' enables ascent. What 'weight' in your life (a responsibility, trial, or difficulty) could be the very thing that lifts you spiritually? What concrete action will you take tomorrow to cooperate with that weight instead of resisting it?
?
Wind gathers clouds and intensifies the cold that brings rain. In what way could a 'cold' or uncomfortable experience you are currently facing be a gathering force for future blessings? Are you willing to endure the cold patiently, trusting that Allah is using it to bring something beneficial? Write down one such cold experience and commit to a dua for patience tonight.

The Four Cardinal Directions of Wind and Their Names
Classifies wind directions into four cardinal points with Arabic names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while wind directions are infinite in reality, scholars have classified them into four cardinal origins with specific Arabic names, and that intermediate winds are called nakba.

Core Idea
Winds are classified into four cardinal directions with specific Arabic names: qabul (east), dabur (west), shamal (north), and janub (south).

The author concludes his discussion of wind by addressing its directional origins. He notes that they are not limited in reality, but scholars have made four primary directions: east, west, north, and south. The Arabs gave specific names to winds from these directions, and winds blowing between them are called nakba. The marginal note further clarifies the linguistic forms of these terms. Let's read the passage and the note.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on wind directions
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the four cardinal points and their Arabic names.
2
Identify the Arab names
The Arabs call winds from east 'qabul', west 'dabur', north 'shamal', south 'janub'.
3
Understand 'nakba'
Winds that blow between these four are called nakba. The marginal note explains that there are four nakba winds, each between two of the cardinal directions.

And its place, so it is not permissible to put it in the place of the genus. Therefore, what we mentioned about the state of smoke in generating wind: most principles of winds are upward, as experience testifies. And wind, as it occurs by this path for the most part, may also occur by the air becoming rarefied, then it pushes away its place by means of the greatness of its quantity, then it pushes what is adjacent to it and it complies, and that adjacent also pushes its adjacent, so the air undulates, and that pushing weakens little by little until an extreme, then it stops. And winds may occur from different directions at once, so those winds push the earthly parts, then the earthly parts become compressed between them, rising as if they twist upon themselves—these are the whirlwinds (زوبعة), plural of زوبعة, which is the wind that rotates upon itself, and the cyclone (إعصار), called in Persian 'bikard bad'. And it has been said: between wind and rain there is opposition and cooperation. As for opposition: because wind most often softens the substance of clouds with its heat and disperses them by its movement, while rain moistens the vapors and joins some of them to others, then they become heavy and cannot rise. So whenever a year has much rain, wind is little, and vice versa. As for cooperation: because rain moistens the earth and restores it so that smoke can rise from it, since moisture helps dissolve dry things and make them ascend, and wind gathers clouds and the coldness of clouds escapes to their interior, so the concentrating cold intensifies. As for the directions of winds, they are not actually limited to a number, but they made their origins four: the points of east, west, north, and south. The Arabs call the winds that blow from them: al-qabul (east wind), al-dabur (west wind), al-shamal (north wind), and al-janub (south wind), and they call the winds that blow between them 'nakba'. The Optical Cause of Halos and Their Weather Signs
Explains optical cause of halos and weather signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how halos around the moon and sun are formed by reflection from moist particles in the atmosphere and how their appearance can indicate upcoming weather changes.

Core Idea
Halos are natural optical reflections from tiny moisture particles, not supernatural omens.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the halo phenomenon around the moon and sun. It details the optical cause involving small, moist, polished particles that reflect light, and describes practical weather signs based on the halo's behavior. The lesson will guide you through the explanation and its observational implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall content. Note the key terms like 'هالة' (halo) and 'رشية' (sprinkled).
The author uses technical optical terms; the translation clarifies them.
2
Analyze the optical cause
Identify the conditions for a halo: moist, polished particles in a thin cloud. Understand how reflection from these tiny mirrors creates a glowing ring without distinct shape.
Compare to a small mirror that only conveys light and color.
3
Explain the invisibility of the central part
The passage explains why the part of the cloud directly facing the moon is not seen: the strong light overwhelms it. Focus on the distinction between seeing the object itself versus its phantom.
4
Learn the weather signs
Note the three signs: tearing apart evenly (clear weather), thickening until disappearance (rain), deviation to one side (wind from that direction). Also consider the double halo case.
These signs are based on empirical observation.
5
Consider the solar halo rarity
Understand why sun halos are rare: the sun's heat dissolves thin clouds. Appreciate Ibn Sina's reported sighting of a rainbow-colored solar halo.
This highlights the exceptional nature of solar halos.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً نقول فقد يحدث في الجو أجزاء رطبة رشية صقيلة كدائرة تحيط تلك الأجزاء بغيم رقيق لطيف لا يحجب ما وراءه عن الأبصار فينعكس منها أي من تلك الأجزاء الواقعة على ذلك الوضع ضوء البصر لصقالتها إلى القمر فيرى في تلك الأجزاء ضوء دون شكله فإن الصقيل الذي ينعكس منه شعاع البصر إذا صغر جداً بحيث لا ينقسم في الحس أدى الضوء واللون دون الشكل والتخطيط كما في المرآة الصغيرة وتلك الأجزاء الرشية مرايا صغار متراصة على هيئة الدائرة فيرى جميع تلك الدائرة كأنها منورة بنور ضعيف وتسمى الهالة وإنما لا يرى الجزء الذي يقابل القمر من ذلك الغيم لأن قوة الشعاع تخفي حجم السحاب الذي لا يستره فلا يرى فيه خيال القمر كيف والشيء إنما يرى على الاستقامة نفسه لا شبحه بخلاف أجزائه التي لا تقابله فإنها تؤدي خيال ضوئه كما عرفت قبل وأكثر ما تتولد الهالة عند عدم الريح فإن تمزقت من جميع الجهات دلت على الصحو وإن ثخن السحاب حتى بطلت دلت على المطر لأن الأجزاء المائية قد كثرت وإن انحرفت من جهة دلت على ريح تأتي من الجهة وإذا اتفق أن توجد سحابتان على الصفة المذكورة إحداهما تحت الأخرى حدثت هناك هالة تحت هالة وتكون التحتانية أعظم لأنها أقرب إلينا وزعم بعضهم أن رأى سبع هالات معاً واعلم أن هالة الشمس وتسمى الطفاوة بضم الطاء نادرة جداً لأن الشمس تحلل السحب الرقيقة ومع ذلك فقد زعم ابن سينا أنه رأى حول الشمس هالة تامة في ألوان قوس قزح ورأى بعد ذلك هالة فيها قوسية قليلة وإنما تنفرج هالة الشمس إذ كثف السحاب وأظلم وحكى أيضاً أنه رأى حول القمر هالة قوسية اللون لأن السحاب كان غليظاً فتقوس في أجزاء الضوء وعرض ما يعرض للقوس
قوله والتخطيط بحسب المقدار والوضع بين أجزاء المرئي
And also we say: there may occur in the atmosphere moist, sprinkled, polished particles, like a circle surrounding those particles around a thin, delicate cloud that does not hide what is behind it from sight. Then the light of sight is reflected from them—i.e., from those particles positioned in that manner—due to their polish to the moon, so he sees in those particles light without its shape. For the polished thing from which the ray of sight is reflected, if it is very small such that it is not divided in sensation, it conveys light and color without shape and outline, as in a small mirror. And those sprinkled particles are small mirrors arranged in the form of a circle, so the whole circle is seen as if illuminated with a weak light, and it is called the halo (الهالة). And the part of that cloud that faces the moon is not seen, because the strength of the ray hides the size of the cloud that does not screen it, so the moon's image is not seen in it. How? For a thing is seen directly itself, not its phantom, unlike its parts that do not face it, for they convey the phantom of its light as you knew before. And most often the halo is generated when there is no wind. If it tears apart from all sides, it indicates clear weather; if the cloud thickens until it disappears, it indicates rain because the watery particles have increased; if it deviates from one side, it indicates wind coming from that side. And if it happens that two clouds exist in the described manner, one under the other, a halo under a halo occurs there, and the lower one is larger because it is closer to us. And some claimed that he saw seven halos together. And know that the halo of the sun, also called الطفاوة with ضمة on the ط, is very rare, because the sun dissolves thin clouds. Yet Ibn Sina claimed that he saw around the sun a complete halo in the colors of the rainbow, and after that he saw a halo with a small arch. And the sun's halo only disperses when the cloud thickens and darkens. And he also narrated that he saw around the moon a halo of rainbow color because the cloud was thick, so it arched in the parts of light and showed what appears to the bow. He said: 'and outline (التخطيط) according to quantity and position among the parts of the visible object.'
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of the halo (الهالة) around the moon and sun. It is a continuous explanation of the cause: moist, polished particles in the atmosphere form a circle around a thin, transparent cloud. The reflection of light from these tiny particles (acting like small mirrors) creates a glowing ring. The author details why the part directly facing the moon is not visible—because the strong light hides it. Then he describes observational signs: if the halo tears apart evenly, it indicates clear weather; if it disappears due to thickening clouds, rain is likely; if it shifts to one side, wind is coming. Two stacked halos can occur, with the lower one larger. The author notes that sun halos are rare but mentions Ibn Sina's sightings. A marginal note clarifies that 'التخطيط' refers to the outline determined by the quantity and arrangement of parts of the seen object.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Halos are supernatural omens or portents.
Correction: Halos are natural optical phenomena caused by reflection of light from tiny moisture particles, and they can indicate weather changes.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

The Rainbow: Optical Cause and Scholarly Debate
Explains the optical cause of the rainbow, variation in colors, and a critique of the explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical optical theory of the rainbow, its necessary conditions, and the scholarly debate surrounding its causes.

Core Idea
The rainbow is a natural optical reflection, not a supernatural sign.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the rainbow (qaws quzah) as a physical phenomenon similar to the halo. It details the conditions for its appearance, the mechanism of reflection, and the variation of colors. The author also presents a critique by al-Hasan al-Farisi and an alternative theory from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, showing the diversity of opinions in Islamic natural philosophy. By studying this passage, you will grasp how classical scholars integrated optics, meteorology, and metaphysics to explain common natural phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the technical terms and the flow of the explanation.
Focus on the initial description of the rainbow's formation.
2
Identify the conditions for the rainbow
List the specific conditions mentioned: particles opposite the sun, a dense background, sun near horizon, observer turning away, etc. Understand each condition's role.
Why is a dense body behind the particles necessary?
3
Understand the reflection mechanism
The text says the ray of sight reflects from the particles to the sun, and due to their smallness, only color (not shape) is perceived. Grasp this optical principle.
Contrast this with how we normally see objects.
4
Examine the color variation explanation
The colors vary according to the particles' own colors, the background (mountains, clouds), and reflected light from dense bodies. Note this empirical observation.
This shows early understanding of differential reflection.
5
Analyze the critique and alternative theories
Read about al-Farisi's rejection and the alternative explanation from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya (celestial conjunctions and spiritual forces). Consider how these reflect different epistemological approaches.
The author defends the majority view while reporting the alternatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يحدث مثل ذلك الذي ذكرناه من الأجزاء الرشية الصقيلة على هيئة الاستدارة في خلاف جهة الشمس وهو قوس قزح وتفصيله أنه إذا وجد في خلاف جهة الشمس أجزاء رشية لطيفة صافية على تلك الهيئة وكان وراءها جسم كثيف إما جبل أو سحاب كدر وكانت الشمس قريبة من الأفق فإذا أدبر على الشمس ونظر إلى تلك الأجزاء انعكس شعاع البصر عنها إلى الشمس ولما كانت صغيرة جدا لم يؤد الشكل بل اللون الذي يكون مركباً من ضوء الشمس ولون المرآة وتختلف ألوانها أي ألوان قوس قزح بحسب اختلاف أجزاء السحاب في ألوانها وبحسب ألوان ما وراءها من الجبال وألوان ما ينعكس منها الضوء من الأجرام الكثيفة ورأيت بعض فضلاء زماننا ممن له في علم المناظر كعب عال وهو المولى الفاضل كمال الملة والدين الحسن الفارسي الله برد مضجعه يدعي بطلان ذلك الذي ذكرناه من أسباب الهالة وقوس قزح لكنه أي ما ذكرناه فيها رأي الجمهور قد ذكرناه متابعة لهم وفي المباحث المشرقية زعم بعضهم أن السبب في حدوث أمثال هذه الحوادث اتصالات فلكية وقوى روحانية اقتضت وجودها وحينئذ لا تكون من قبيل الخيالات وهو أن يرى صورة شيء مع صورة شيء آخر مظهر له
قوله وهي قوس قزح يقال قزح الكلب بوله ورشه وقوس قزح التي في السماء غير متصرفة كذا في الصحاح وقوله وهو أن يرى إلخ أي ما يكون من قبيل الخيالات هو أن يرى وقوله وهذا الذي ذكره ذلك البعض وقوله لكن هذا الوجه الذي ذكره ذلك البعض
And something similar to what we mentioned of the polished sprinkled particles in the form of a circle may occur opposite the direction of the sun, and it is the rainbow (قوس قزح). Its detail: when opposite the direction of the sun there exist delicate, clear, sprinkled particles in that form, and behind them is a dense body, either a mountain or a dark cloud, and the sun is near the horizon. Then if one turns away from the sun and looks at those particles, the ray of sight is reflected from them to the sun, and since they are very small, it does not convey shape but rather the color which is composed of sunlight and the color of the mirror. And their colors, i.e., the colors of the rainbow, vary according to the difference of the cloud parts in their colors, and according to the colors of what is behind them of mountains, and the colors of the dense bodies from which light is reflected. And I saw some virtuous contemporaries of our time, among those who have a high standing in optics—he is the excellent master Kamal al-Milla wa al-Din al-Hasan al-Farisi, may God refresh his resting place—claiming the falsity of what we mentioned concerning the causes of the halo and the rainbow. But what we mentioned about them is the opinion of the majority; we mentioned it following them. And in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: some claimed that the cause of the occurrence of such phenomena is celestial conjunctions and spiritual forces that necessitate their existence, and then they are not of the category of illusions. And it is to see an image of something together with an image of another thing that manifests it. He said: 'And it (i.e., قوس قزح) is the rainbow; it is said: قزح الكلب بوله ورشه. And قوس قزح in the sky is indeclinable, as in al-Sihah.' And he said: 'And that is to see, etc.' i.e., what is of the category of illusions is to see. And he said: 'And this is what that person mentioned.' And he said: 'But this aspect that that person mentioned.'
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage explains the rainbow as a similar phenomenon to the halo, occurring when polished moisture particles are arranged in a circle opposite the sun. The conditions include a dense background (mountain or dark cloud) and the sun near the horizon. The observer turns away from the sun and sees the reflected light from these tiny particles, which convey color but not shape. The colors vary based on the cloud particles' colors, the background, and reflected light. The author then notes a critique by al-Hasan al-Farisi, who rejected this explanation, but the author defends it as the majority view. He also mentions an alternative theory from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that attributes such phenomena to celestial conjunctions and spiritual forces. Marginal notes clarify the term 'قوس قزح' and the discussion of illusions.

The rainbow is an optical reflection from moisture particles opposite the sun, with colors varying due to environmental factors, and its cause was debated between natural philosophers and metaphysicians.

What 'dense bodies' in your life might be distorting how you see reality? Will you take a concrete step this week to identify and correct one such distortion in your understanding of deen?

The Cause of the Rainbow
Explains the formation and color variation of the rainbow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical explanation of how rainbows are formed according to classical Islamic science, including the roles of reflected light, fine droplets, and background conditions.

Core Idea
Rainbows are optical illusions caused by reflection from fine droplets.

In this passage, the author explains that the rainbow is not a physically existing arc but an optical phenomenon. He describes the precise conditions needed for its formation: fine polished droplets opposite the sun, a dense background, and the observer turning away from the sun. Understanding this explanation reveals how classical scholars integrated empirical observation with causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's description of the rainbow come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by connecting to a previous discussion about similar phenomena.
2
Identify the conditions for rainbow formation
The passage lists several conditions: fine polished particles opposite the sun, a dense body behind them, the sun near the horizon, and the observer with back to the sun. List these conditions.
These conditions all work together to produce the rainbow.
3
Understand the role of reflection
The author explains that the ray of sight reflects from the droplets to the sun. Because the droplets are small, only color is perceived, not shape.
This reflection process is key to the optical illusion.
4
Examine the explanation for color variation
The passage says colors differ based on the colors of cloud particles, background mountains, and other dense bodies. Think about how this fits with the idea of light composition.
The author attributes color to a combination of sunlight and the 'color of the mirror'.
5
Consider the common misconception
Many people think the rainbow is a solid arch. Compare this with the author's explanation that it is an optical effect.
This helps clarify the author's methodological approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يحدث مثل ذلك الذي ذكرناه من الأجزاء الرشية الصقيلة على هيئة الاستدارة في خلاف جهة الشمس وهو قوس قزح وتفصيله أنه إذا وجد في خلاف جهة الشمس أجزاء رشية لطيفة صافية على تلك الهيئة وكان وراءها جسم كثيف إما جبل أو سحاب كدر وكانت الشمس قريبة من الأفق فإذا أدبر على الشمس ونظر إلى تلك الأجزاء انعكس شعاع البصر عنها إلى الشمس ولما كانت صغيرة جدا لم يؤد الشكل بل اللون الذي يكون مركباً من ضوء الشمس ولون المرآة وتختلف ألوانها بحسب اختلاف أجزاء السحاب في ألوانها وبحسب ألوان ما وراءها من الجبال وألوان ما ينعكس منها الضوء من الأجرام الكثيفة
And something similar may occur from the fine polished particles in the shape of circularity opposite the direction of the sun, and it is the rainbow. Its detail is that if there exist opposite the direction of the sun fine clear particles in that shape, and behind them is a dense body, either a mountain or a dark cloud, and the sun is near the horizon, then if one turns his back to the sun and looks at those particles, the ray of sight reflects from them to the sun. Since they are very small, they do not convey the shape but convey the color, which is composed of sunlight and the color of the mirror. Their colors differ according to the difference of the cloud parts in their colors, and according to the colors of what is behind them of mountains, and the colors of what the light reflects from them of dense bodies.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage explains the cause of the rainbow (قوس قزح). The author describes it as resulting from fine polished droplets opposite the sun, with a dark background. When the observer turns away from the sun, light reflects from the droplets to the eye. The droplets are too small to show an image, so only color appears, composed of sunlight and the droplet's own color. The rainbow's colors vary depending on the cloud particles' colors, the background mountains, and the reflective dense bodies.

Key Terms
قوس قزح
انعكاس
أجزاء رشية
لون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rainbow is a physically existing arch in the sky.
Correction: The rainbow is an optical illusion caused by reflection of light from fine water droplets; it does not exist as a solid object.

Checkpoint
What is the role of the dense body behind the droplets in rainbow formation?
A) It blocks sunlight
B) It reflects light
C) It provides color
D) It serves as a dark background

Key Takeaway
The rainbow results from reflection of sunlight off fine droplets opposite the sun, with colors depending on droplet and background properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What step can you take today to seek a more objective understanding of a situation?
?
If the rainbow is an illusion created by light and droplets, what other 'solid' realities in your life might be illusions? Are you willing to question your assumptions and seek the underlying causes, even when it is uncomfortable?

Alternative Theory: Celestial and Psychic Causes
Reports an alternative causal theory from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative causal explanation for atmospheric phenomena based on celestial connections and psychic forces, and how it differs from the theory of optical illusions.

Core Idea
Some phenomena may be caused by real celestial and psychic forces, not just illusions.

This lesson introduces a distinct theory from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya that challenges the purely optical explanation. Here, the author reports that some scholars attribute phenomena like halos and rainbows to genuine celestial connections and psychic forces, not to mere images or illusions. We will examine this alternative and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Focus on the claim being made.
Notice that the passage begins with 'In al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya' — this signals a reported view.
2
Identify key terms
Look at the terms 'اتصالات فلكية' (celestial connections) and 'قوى روحانية' (psychic forces). What do they suggest about the cause?
The passage contrasts these with 'خيالات' (illusions).
3
Compare with the optical theory
Think about how this theory differs from the previous explanation that phenomena are illusions. Why would someone propose this alternative?
This theory claims the phenomena are real existences, not just images of something else.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المباحث المشرقية: زعم بعضهم أن السبب في حدوث أمثال هذه الحوادث اتصالات فلكية وقوى روحانية اقتضت وجودها، وحينئذ لا تكون من قبيل الخيالات وهو أن يرى صورة شيء مع صورة شيء آخر مظهر له

Some scholars argue that phenomena like halos and rainbows are not mere optical illusions but are caused by celestial alignments (اتصالات فلكية) and psychic forces (قوى روحانية). According to this view, what we see are real entities, not just reflections or refractions. The author contrasts this with the previous optical explanation, noting that the alternative considers these phenomena as genuine existences, not as images of something else.

The Mechanics of Springs: Vacuum and Vapor Theory
Explains the formation of flowing and stagnant springs through vapor and vacuum theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic scholars explained the formation of flowing and stagnant springs using the concepts of confined vapor, the impossibility of a vacuum, and the alternative view based on vapor abundance.

Core Idea
Springs flow by the chain of vapor turning to water and being replaced, or by the force of abundant vapors.

In this passage, al-Jurjani explains the natural mechanism behind flowing and stagnant springs. He presents two competing theories: one based on the impossibility of a vacuum, and another from Imam al-Razi based on the abundance of vapor. Understanding this will help you see how medieval Islamic science combined observation with rational principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the description of vapor confined in the earth and how it produces water.
Pay attention to the terms 'بخار' and 'امتناع الخلاء'.
2
Identify the two explanations
The first part explains flowing springs via vacuum replacement. The second part summarizes al-Razi's alternative based on vapor abundance.
Notice al-Jurjani's comment 'فتأمل' at the end.
3
Contrast the two views
Compare how each view explains flowing vs stagnant water. The vacuum view emphasizes necessity; the vapor view emphasizes force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالبخار المحتقن في الأرض يخرج القليل من مسامها وينقلب الكثير بمعونة البرد الذي في باطن الأرض ماء ويشفها فيخرج منها ومنه العيون السيالة إذا كان البخار كثيراً فحصل المدد بعد المدد كان الفائض يحدث الثاني ضرورة امتناع الخلاء فإن البخار الذي انقلب ماء وفاض إلى وجه الأرض وجب أن ينجذب إلى مكانه ما يقوم مقامه لئلا يكون خلاء فينقلب هو أيضاً ماء ويفيض، وهكذا يستنبع كل جزء منه جزءاً آخر. قال الإمام الرازي : ومياه العيون الراكدة تحدث من أبخرة بلغت من قوتها أن اندفعت إلى وجه الأرض ولكن لم تبلغ من كثرة مددها وقوتها أن يطرد تاليها سابقها وهذا الكلام ينافي ما ذكره المصنف من التعليل بامتناع الخلاء ويقتضي أن يعلل السيلان بكثرة الأبخرة المقتضية للاندفاع إلى فوق والركود بقلتها، فتأمل . قال : ومياه القنى والآبار متولدة من أبخرة ناقصة القوة عن أن تشق الأرض فإذا أزيل ثقل الأرض عن وجهها صادفت تندفع إليه بأدنى حركة، فإن لم يحصل هناك مسيل فهو البئر وإن حصل فهو القناة ونسبة القنى إلى الآبار كنسبة العيون السيالة إلى الراكدة. واعلم أن النزح من الآبار والعيون الراكدة سبب لنبوع الماء فيها لأن ثقل الماء الظاهر يمنع سائر الأبخرة عن الظهور فإذا نزح قويت تلك الأبخرة واندفعت إلى خارج وفيها اختلفوا في أن هذه المياه متولدة من أجزاء مائية متفرقة في عمق الأرض إذا اجتمعت، أو من الهواء البخاري الذي ينقلب ماء وهذا الثاني وإن كان ممكناً إلا أن الأول أولى لأن مياه العيون والقنوات والآبار تزيد بزيادة الثلوج والأمطار
The vapor confined in the earth—a little of it comes out of its pores, and much of it turns into water with the help of the cold inside the earth, and it oozes out of it. From this come flowing springs, if the vapor is abundant and supply comes after supply, then the overflowing causes the second necessarily because of the impossibility of a vacuum. For when the vapor that turned into water overflows to the surface of the earth, it is necessary that something should be attracted to its place to replace it so that there is no vacuum, and it also turns into water and overflows. Thus each part draws forth another part. Imam al-Razi said: Stagnant spring waters arise from vapors that have enough force to push to the surface but not enough abundance and force to expel the succeeding one. This statement contradicts what the author (al-Iji) mentioned about the impossibility of a vacuum, and it requires that flowing be explained by the abundance of vapors that require upward motion, and stagnation by their scarcity. So reflect. He said: Waters of canals and wells are generated from vapors that are too weak to split the earth. When the weight of the earth is removed from above them, they find a way and push to it with the slightest motion. If no channel is formed, it is a well; if a channel is formed, it is a canal. The relation of canals to wells is like that of flowing springs to stagnant ones. Know that drawing water from wells and stagnant springs is a cause for water to spring forth, because the weight of the existing water prevents other vapors from emerging. When water is drawn, those vapors become strong and push outward. People differ over whether these waters are generated from scattered watery parts in the depth of the earth that gather, or from vaporous air that turns into water. The second is possible, but the first is more likely because the waters of springs, canals, and wells increase with the increase of snow and rain.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage explains the formation of groundwater sources. The author (al-Jurjani) describes vapor confined in the earth: a little escapes through pores, but most turns into water due to underground cold and emerges as springs. Continuous flow is explained by the impossibility of a vacuum (امتناع الخلاء): as water flows out, vapor moves to replace it and also turns to water, creating a chain. He then cites Imam al-Razi's distinction between flowing springs (العيون السيالة) and stagnant springs (العيون الراكدة). Al-Razi's explanation relies on the force of vapors: abundant vapors cause flow, scarcity causes stagnation. Al-Jurjani notes this contradicts the vacuum explanation and advises reflection. Waters of canals (قنى) and wells (آبار) come from weaker vapors that cannot break the surface; when the overburden is removed (by digging), they emerge. Drawing water reduces the weight of existing water, allowing more vapor to push up. Finally, there is a dispute: whether these waters originate from pre-existing water particles that gather, or from vapor turning into water. The author favors the first because rainfall increases spring waters.

Key Terms
بخار
امتناع الخلاء
عيون سيالة
عيون راكدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All springs are produced solely by the vacuum principle.
Correction: Al-Razi's alternative shows that abundance of vapor can also cause flow independently of vacuum.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what causes water to stagnate in a spring?
A) The water is too cold.
B) The vapor does not have enough force to push out the preceding water.
C) The vacuum is too strong.
D) The earth's pores are blocked.

Key Takeaway
The explanation of spring formation involves two competing theories: the vacuum-driven chain reaction and the vapor-abundance model, illustrating the diversity of scientific reasoning in Islamic thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter two competing explanations for a natural phenomenon, like the vacuum theory and the vapor theory of springs, which one do you instinctively prefer? Are you willing to hold both as possible, or do you feel the need to reject one? What does your reaction reveal about your intellectual humility before Allah's creation?
?
The author ends with 'فتأمل' — reflect. When was the last time you paused to deeply reflect on a simple natural process, like the flow of a spring, and considered the multiple layers of causation Allah has placed in it? Commit to spending five minutes today observing one natural phenomenon and asking: 'What are the possible causes, and how do they point to the Creator?'
?
If both theories can be partially true under different conditions, does that unsettle you? Are you comfortable with complexity and uncertainty in understanding Allah's creation, or do you demand a single, simple answer? How might this comfort or discomfort affect your relationship with divine wisdom embedded in the natural world?

Wells, Canals, and the Origin of Groundwater
Explains the formation of canals and wells and the origin of groundwater.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how canals and wells are formed from weak vapors, why drawing water increases flow, and the dispute over the origin of groundwater.

Core Idea
Weak vapors unable to break the surface require removal of earth weight to form canals and wells.

After discussing springs, al-Jurjani turns to the formation of canals (قنى) and wells (آبار). He explains that these come from vapors too weak to split the earth, but when the overburden is removed by digging, they emerge. He also discusses the effect of drawing water and the scholarly dispute about whether groundwater originates from pre-existing water particles or from vapor condensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic from 'قال : ومياه القنى والآبار' to the end. Notice the comparison to springs.
Key terms: قنى, آبار.
2
Understand the formation mechanism
The passage explains that weak vapors cannot break the earth; when the weight of earth is removed, they push out. This produces a well if no channel, or a canal if a channel exists.
Note the analogy to flowing vs stagnant springs.
3
Analyze the effect of drawing water
Drawing water reduces the weight of existing water, allowing more vapor to emerge. This explains why wells replenish.
4
Consider the dispute on water origin
The author presents two views: water from scattered particles, or from vapor turning to water. He favors the first because rainfall increases water in springs and wells.
This connects empirical observation to theoretical preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Drawing water from a well creates new water out of nothing.
Correction: Drawing water allows previously trapped vapors to emerge by removing the weight of the existing water.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given for why drawing water from a well increases the flow?
A) It creates a vacuum.
B) It reduces the weight of the water above, allowing vapors to push out.
C) It cools the earth and condenses more vapor.
D) It opens new pores in the earth.

Key Takeaway
Canals and wells form from weak vapors that emerge when the overlying earth is removed; the replenishment of water upon drawing is explained by the removal of water weight allowing more vapor to push out, and the origin of groundwater is debated between particle aggregation and vapor condensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author explains that drawing water from a well actually allows more water to come forth. In your life, what are you holding onto so tightly that by letting go (giving, releasing) you might invite greater abundance? When will you take the first step to 'draw' from that well?
?
The passage says weak vapors need the removal of earth's weight to emerge. What 'weights' of pride, comfort, or fear are pressing on your soul, keeping your hidden strengths from surfacing? What specific weight will you ask Allah to help you remove this week?
?
The author favors the view that groundwater increases with rain and snow. Do you live as if your spiritual refreshment depends on external sources (like the rain of guidance) or do you assume you are self-sufficient? What does this reveal about your daily dependence on Allah?

The Cause of Earthquakes
Attributes earthquakes to trapped vapour and smoke moving within the earth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical explanation of earthquakes as resulting from trapped vapor and smoke attempting to escape from the earth.

Core Idea
Earthquakes are caused by trapped gases struggling to escape through compacted earth.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a naturalistic account of earthquakes rooted in the physics of vapors and smoke. Before the modern theory of plate tectonics, scholars like al-Jurjani explained seismic activity as the result of subterranean gases pushing against dense pores. The author links the frequency of earthquakes to the porosity of the ground and human interventions like digging wells.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: بخار (vapor), دخان (smoke), مسام (pores), and زلازل (earthquakes).
The passage is concise but packed with causal reasoning.
2
Identify the conditions for earthquakes
Notice the two necessary conditions for an earthquake: abundant vapor/smoke and densely compacted pores. Then see the opposite conditions: scarcity of gases or open pores prevent earthquakes.
3
Apply the explanation to real examples
The author gives two examples: soft ground has fewer earthquakes because pores are open, and hard ground with many wells has fewer earthquakes because wells provide escape routes.
Think about how this explains regional variations in seismic activity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبخار والدخان اللذان في الأرض قد يكثران ويزيدان الخروج منها بقوة ومسامها متكاثفة فيزلزلانها بحركتيهما ومنه تتكون الزلازل وإذا كانا قليلين أو كان مسامها مفتوحة لم يكن زلزلة، ولذلك قلت : الزلازل في الأراضي الرخوة وإذا كثرت الآبار والقنى في أرض صلبة قلت زلزلتها
The vapor and smoke in the earth may become abundant and increase their exit from it with force while its pores are densely compacted, so they shake it with their motions, and from this earthquakes are formed. If they are few, or if its pores are open, there is no earthquake. For this reason, earthquakes are few in soft ground, and if wells and canals become plentiful in hard ground, its earthquake is reduced.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage explains the cause of earthquakes (الزلازل). Vapor (بخار) and smoke (دخان) trapped inside the earth accumulate and try to escape with force. If the earth's pores are densely compacted (متكاثفة), the trapped gases cause shaking. If they are few or the pores are open, no earthquake occurs. This explains why earthquakes are rare in soft ground (الأراضي الرخوة), as soft soil allows gases to escape easily. Similarly, in hard ground, digging many wells and canals (الآبار والقنى) provides escape routes, reducing earthquakes.

Key Terms
بخار
دخان
زلازل
مسام
أراضي رخوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Earthquakes are caused solely by underground water movement.
Correction: The text states that earthquakes are caused by vapor and smoke, not water.

Checkpoint
Why are earthquakes less frequent in soft ground?
A) Soft ground absorbs shock
B) Pores are open allowing gases to escape
C) There is less vapor
D) The ground is too weak to shake

Key Takeaway
Earthquakes result from the forceful escape of trapped vapors and smoke when earth's pores are densely compacted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of an earthquake, do you instinctively think of God's punishment or of natural processes? Why does your mind go there first, and what does this reveal about your reliance on secondary causes?
?
If you fully believed that every calamity has a physical explanation that you could understand, would you still turn to God in desperation during such events? Where does your trust truly lie?
?
The author didn't invoke the supernatural here—he gave a natural cause. Does that make the earthquake less awe-inspiring or less a sign of God? Examine your heart: do you need miracles to feel God's presence, or can you see Him in the orderly workings of His creation?

Springs and Wells from Confined Vapors
Al-Jurjani explains how confined vapors produce springs and wells.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani and al-Razi explain the generation of springs, wells, and channels from confined vapors, including the role of the impossibility of vacuum and the debate on water origin.

Core Idea
Confined vapor turns to water and flows, driven by the impossibility of a vacuum.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a philosophical account of springs and wells based on Aristotelian physics. He explains how vapor confined in the earth turns into water and flows, with the key mechanism being the avoidance of a vacuum. The passage also includes al-Razi's critique and alternative explanation based on vapor strength, and discusses wells and channels. This lesson will guide you through the core ideas and the internal debate among scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the sequence: how vapor becomes water, then flows, and how the flow is sustained.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two explanations
Notice that the author first explains flowing springs using the impossibility of vacuum (al-khalāʼ). Then al-Razi critiques this and suggests an alternative based on the strength of vapors. Distinguish these two causal mechanisms.
This shows a live debate within Islamic philosophy.
3
Examine wells and channels
Read the description of wells (ābār) and channels (qanāt). Note how the strength of vapor determines whether it can split the earth or waits for excavation.
Compare with the earlier explanation for springs.
4
Reflect on the philosophical context
Consider that this is not empirical science but a theoretical model consistent with Aristotelian elements (earth, water, air, fire) and the concept of vacuum avoidance. Ask yourself how this differs from modern hydrology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الخامس من المركبات البخار والدخان

كالمرآة في ظن أن الصورة الأولى حاصلة في الشيء الثاني ولا يكون فيه بحسب نفس الأمر . قال الإمام الرازي: وهذا الذي ذكره لا ينافي ما ذكرناه فإن الصحة والمرض قد يستندان إلى أسباب عنصرية تارة وإلى اتصالات فلكية وتأثيرات نفسانية أخرى، لكن هذا الوجه يؤيده أن أصحاب التجارب شهدوا بأن أمثال هذه الحوادث في الجو تدل على حدوث حوادث في الأرض، فلولا أنها موجودات مستندة إلى تلك الاتصالات والأوضاع لم يستمر هذا الاستدلال ( وأيضاً نقول : ( فالبخار المحتقن في الأرض ) يخرج القليل من مسامها وينقلب الكثير بمعونة البرد الذي في باطن الأرض ( ماء ويشفها ) فيخرج منها ( ومنه العيون ) السيالة ( إذا كان البخار كثيراً فحصل المدد بعد المدد كان الفائض يحدث الثاني ضرورة امتناع (الخلاء فإن البخار الذي انقلب ماء وفاض إلى وجه الأرض وجب أن ينجذب إلى مكانه ما يقوم مقامه لئلا يكون خلاء فينقلب هو أيضاً ماء ويفيض، وهكذا يستنبع كل جزء منه جزءاً آخر. قال الإمام الرازي : ومياه العيون الراكدة تحدث من أبخرة بلغت من قوتها أن اندفعت إلى وجه الأرض ولكن لم تبلغ من كثرة مددها وقوتها أن يطرد تاليها سابقها وهذا الكلام ينافي ما ذكره المصنف من التعليل بامتناع الخلاء ويقتضي أن يعلل السيلان بكثرة الأبخرة المقتضية للاندفاع إلى فوق والركود بقلتها، فتأمل . قال : ومياه القنى والآبار متولدة من أبخرة ناقصة القوة عن أن تشق الأرض فإذا أزيل ثقل الأرض عن وجهها صادفت تندفع إليه بأدنى حركة، فإن لم يحصل هناك مسيل فهو البئر وإن حصل فهو القناة ونسبة القنى إلى الآبار كنسبة العيون السيالة إلى الراكدة. واعلم أن النزح من الآبار والعيون الراكدة سبب لنبوع الماء فيها لأن ثقل الماء الظاهر يمنع سائر الأبخرة عن الظهور فإذا نزح قويت تلك الأبخرة واندفعت إلى خارج وفيها اختلفوا في أن هذه المياه متولدة من أجزاء مائية متفرقة في عمق الأرض إذا اجتمعت، أو من الهواء البخاري الذي ينقلب ماء وهذا الثاني وإن كان ممكناً إلا أن الأول أولى لأن مياه العيون والقنوات والآبار تزيد بزيادة الثلوج والأمطار.
The First Observatory - Fifth Section: On the composites vapor and smoke. Like the mirror in the supposition that the first image is present in the second thing but is not in it in reality. Imam al-Razi said: This that he mentioned does not contradict what we mentioned, for health and disease may sometimes be based on elemental causes and sometimes on celestial conjunctions and other psychic influences, but this aspect is supported by the fact that experts in experimentation testified that similar events in the atmosphere indicate the occurrence of events on earth; if they were not existents dependent on those conjunctions and positions, this inference would not hold. And also we say: The vapor confined in the earth — a little of it exits from its pores and most of it, with the help of the cold in the earth's interior, turns into water and seeps through it, so it emerges from it, and from it come flowing springs. If the vapor is abundant and reinforcement follows after reinforcement, the overflowing one produces the second due to the impossibility of vacuum. For the vapor that turned into water and flowed to the earth's surface, what takes its place must be attracted to its place to avoid a vacuum, so it also turns into water and flows, and thus each part draws forth another part. Imam al-Razi said: The waters of stagnant springs arise from vapors that were so strong that they pushed to the earth's surface but did not reach, due to the abundance and strength of their reinforcements, that their successor drives out their predecessor. This statement contradicts what the author mentioned of causation by the impossibility of vacuum and requires that flow be caused by the abundance of vapors necessitating upward thrust, and stagnation by their scarcity. Reflect. He said: The waters of channels and wells are generated from vapors that are too weak to split the earth; so when the weight of the earth is removed from their face, they find it and push towards it with the slightest motion. If no channel occurs, it is a well; if it occurs, it is a channel. The relation of channels to wells is like the relation of flowing springs to stagnant ones. Know that drawing water from wells and stagnant springs is a cause for water gushing in them, because the apparent weight of water prevents other vapors from appearing; so when water is drawn, those vapors become strong and push outward. And in this they differed: whether these waters are generated from scattered aqueous parts in the depth of the earth when they gather, or from the vaporous air that turns into water. This second, though possible, the first is more likely because the waters of springs, channels, and wells increase with an increase in snow and rain.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a physical explanation within the philosophers' framework, describing how vapors confined in the earth generate springs, wells, and channels. The passage begins by citing Imam al-Razi's comment that the previous account does not contradict the influence of elemental and celestial causes. Then it presents the main argument: confined vapor turns into water with the help of cold; if there is abundant vapor, continuous flow is maintained by the impossibility of a vacuum (al-khalāʼ). Each portion of water that flows out is replaced by the vapor that turns into water, ensuring continuity. Imam al-Razi then critiques this vacuum-based explanation, noting that for stagnant springs, the explanation should be based on the force of vapors rather than vacuum avoidance. The passage continues with an explanation of wells and channels: vapors too weak to split the earth will flow into an excavated channel or well. Finally, it discusses the effect of drawing water (al-nazḥ) in stimulating further flow, and the debate over whether water originates from scattered subsurface particles or from converted vapor, favoring the former because waters increase with rain and snow.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage presents an empirical scientific explanation.
Correction: It is a philosophical account within Aristotelian physics, not based on modern empirical methods, and it includes internal critique.

Checkpoint
According to the explanation in this passage, what is the primary reason for the continuous flow of a flowing spring?
A) The weight of water pushing downward
B) The impossibility of a vacuum in the earth
C) The heat from the earth's interior
D) The attraction of celestial bodies

Key Takeaway
The generation of springs was explained by the transformation of confined vapor into water, with flow maintained by the impossibility of vacuum, though al-Razi offered an alternative based on vapor strength.

?
Al-Jurjani argued based on the impossibility of vacuum, but al-Razi pointed out contradictions and offered an alternative based on observation. ?
The passage notes that waters increase with snow and rain, a simple empirical observation.
